This eminent Specialist, Founder, and Chief
Surgeon of the long-established and favourably-
known Dispensary that now bears his name, re-
ceived his medical education in the leading colleges
and hospitals of Europe. He has devoted a life-
time to the study of
PRIVATE, NERVOUS, AND SKIN
DISEASES.
He has by profound research thoroughly investi-
gated their origin, traced their deadly sources and
insidious dissemination, and ascertained the surest
and safest methods by which they may be eradi-
cated, with these widely recognised qualifications
combined with unswerving faithfulness to his
patients, he has during his practice, extending
over FORTY YEARS, attained a well-deserved
pre-eminence in their treatment. His practice
embraces diseases which probably cause more un-
happiness than any other class of troubles that
afflict mankind.
YOUNG MEN
who suffer from the results of early follies, trans-
gressions or excesses of more nature age, bladder
difficulties, a whitish or dark ropy sediment in
the water, accompanied by slight burning or
smarting sensation, Headaches. Drowsiness during
the day, circles round the eyes, Giddiness,
irregularity of the bowels, specks before the eyes,
bashfulness, discontentedness, weakness or pains
in the back. Liver or kidney complaints, dreams,
ouzzing noises in the ears and head, pimples, a
fear that something dreadful is about to happen,
weak stomach, yellow or blood-shot eyes, impure
blood, hasty, uncon reliable temper,, hacking
cough, who cannot marry, nervousness in society,
wakefulness, palpitation of the heart, general
weakness, and other symptoms which lead to in-
sanity and death, should consult him, for he has
ma a tered these difficulties. On receipt of fid. in
stamps he will send secure from observation in a
plain wrapper, postage
FREE, A LARGE CLOTH-BOUND
BOOK.
Showing plainly how all diseases of the private or
nervous system may be cured thoroughly and per-
manently without unnecessary expence. This
book will prove a valuable companion, and even
though you be perfectly well its general inform-
tion and prescription may assist you in restoring
some poor friend to health even after his case has
been pronounced incurable by his medical at-
tendant.

YOU S DEBILIT Y. Should you require medical
treatment, as procrastination is sometimes dan-
gerous, it would be advisable for you to sit down
quietly in your chamber and write to the doctor,
detailing every experienced symptom. He will
on receipt of such a letter, write you, promptly
and courteously, a candid and straightforward
oponion of your case. If you do not think you
understand the case, send for his famous medical
work, the price of which is but sixpence; enclosed
in the volume will be found a list of carefully-pre-
pared questions, which will, in all probability,
assist you materially in describing your ailment.
Dr. Richards uses nothing but the purest remedies,
and never prescribes mercury under any circum-
stances. The moment letters are carefully per-
used and answered they are destroyed by fire,
and patients may rest assured that their cases
will remain a secret between the doctor and
themselves. Dr. Richards has successfully treated
nearly 25,000 cases since coming to Australia, and
has permission from at least 10,000 persons to
show their written evidence of his success. All
communications should be addressed

SATURDAY, MAY 2, 1891.
The Governor-General.
The travail of the late Federal Convention
will not have been in vain if it enables
Australians to see who among the delegates
are the true Democrats, and who spurious.
The voting regarding the appointment of a
Governor-General was, in the truest sense,
one-sided, if only from the fact that all but
one sat on the same side and met Sir George
Grey’s amendment with a stolid non pos-
sumus. What, then, was Grey’s proposal
that it obtained such a short shrift, even
from some who might have been expected to
support it ? Merely that Australians should
not be shut out from the highest office in
their own commonwealth —that the mis-
fortune of being Australians should be
no bar sinister to their eligibility for the
supreme post in the country’s executive, as
the misfortune of being a Celt or a Papist is
to a man’s fitness to hold a second-hand
court at Dublin Castle. The division on the
question was an object-lesson in comicality.
The sole “yea’’came from a veteran Eng-
lishman who had done much more than all
the other delegates combined to hold the
Empire together, while the most vociferous
“nays” were belched by many who were
born in Australia and knew nothing of the
great world outside except by hearsay.
When the Jews of Samuel’s day clamoured
for a king to “go out before them ” they, at
least, insisted on having one of their own
people crowned. But when the Australians
of to-day solicit, not a monarch but a
monarch’s simulacrum, they demand that he
should be supplied from a little island the
size of Victoria situated at the other side of
the world. Thus the first essential to be-
coming a chief Viceroy in the future will be
to know nothing of the land whose destinies
you are deputed to guide. Perhaps excep-
tion may be made in favour of persons
who, like Mr. Henniker Heaton and
others, take the precaution of return-
ing to England and remaining there
long enough to cleanse themselves from
antipodean sympathies. An antagonism to
Irish local government and a plethoric purse,
by conferring on such persons the right to
wear a coronet, may, at the same time, lift
them into the rank of eligibles for an Aus-
tralian Governor-Generalship. But in the
face of the man residing in Sydney, Mel-
bourne, or Brisbane, who wishes to occupy
the vice-regal palace, the door is to be as
severely slammed as that of the Inferno in
the faces of unfortunates desirous of chang-
ing their lodgings.
# * #
Thus Sir George Grey :
I believe in my own mind it is essential for you that
every one of your officers be selected by the people of
this country. Even in the case of the Governor-Genera!,
I believe the people ought to have the right of choosing
who that man should be. Let them choose from England
if they please, or from any part of the world ; but no
nation can be perfect unless its people ha\ e every office
open to their ambition, unless it be known tint the really
o-i-eat and good men of their country can vise to the very
highest position in the country and exercise the highest
duties in it. . . . And to say that no man in Australia
shall have an advantage of that sort, is an absolute cruelty
to the people of Australia.
From the foregoing excerpts it is obvious
that the illustrious Maorilancl delegate, for
all his knowledge of books and of men, did
not grasp the situation—did not understand
the peculiarities of Australian Democracy,
or, rather, of those who travesty it. Much
as Tom Moohe dearly loved a lord so the
typical Australian democrat is never quite at
his ease unless he has a peer before whom to
prostrate himself. “ Get off your knees,
d you, and rob your churches,” bawled
Mark Twain as the Italian beggars on the
steps of their gorgeous cathedrals whined
for alms. Similarly the man of independent
mind feels prompted to shout to his delegates
whether in Parliament or Convention, “Stand
steady, my good men, and don’t let your
knees knock together at the prospect of see-
ing the highest office in the land occupied
by an Australian Commoner. An untitled
fellow-citizen is good enough for sixty-five
millions of the world’s most advanced people.
He is surely good enough for you.” Of a
truth it might be asserted that if cutting
the painter in the immediate future brought
some anxieties and even dangers in its wake
they would be amply repaid by the lesson in
self-respect and self-reliance such an event
would teach, as well as by the discomfiture
of the parasites who, by their servility, make
the very name of Australia malodorous in
plain men’s nostrils. In the whole of the
Constitution of the United States there is no
more admirable provision, none that has
worked so beneficently—so far as any pro-
vision can work in a society founded on the
false doctrine of Individualism, instead of
on the rock of progressive Socialism —as that
which proclaims: “No titles of nobility
shall be granted by the United States ; and
no person holding any office of profit or
trust under them shall, without the consent
of Congress, accept any title of any kind
whatever from any king, prince, or any
foreign State.” Had the Australian Federal
delegates incorporated the foregoing, word
for word, in their draft, merely substituting
the Commonwealth for the States ; and had
they completed the paragraph by the ad-
dendum : “To the Australian people alone
belongs the right of electing the Common-
wealth’s Governor-General or President,”
popular apathy or suspicion would quickly
give way to satisfaction. But with one
honourable exception the delegates could not
rise to the occasion. The patriotic lymph
that would destroy the deep-seated tubercles
of political and social sycophancy has yet to
be discovered. So it comes that, should the
Convention result in action, a country
almost as large as Europe may have to
accept for its local fountain of honour a
titled British card-sharper, possibly nomi-
nated by an elderly British roue.
Some astute person may suggest that though
the Crown hold the nomination and Aus-
tralians are virtually excluded, that need not
necessarily result in the sending of a peer to
preside. An able and elderly commoner
might be chosen. Such might have been
the case once. Indeed, the time is not
so long gone by since a naval captain,
fresh from the quarter-deck, would have
been received with acclaim, while a
major-general on the retired list, whose
commands never extended beyond the
parade-ground and the halberds, would
have evoked enthusiasm. That time, how-
ever, has slipped away, and to the bluff
martinet of the past has succeeded the
affluent alleged noble of the present. The
very proposal in these times to supply
Sydney or Melbourne with a Governor
without a place in the peerage, would be
received with a chorus of anger and in-
credulity by the bourgeoisie of two provinces.
Conservative journals, whose proprietors
would pawn the soiled linen of their souls
for a bow from a baron, and radical organs
of boisterous iconoclasm, would quickly find
themselves joining the hue-and-cry of re-
tired food and drink adulterators against the
British administration that presumed to send
an untitled Jones when the services of a
titled Smith were at its disposal. It might bo
that Jones was a statesman, and irreproach-
able. How insignificant in the balance were
such commonplace attributes when a coronet
was in the other scale ! The English Glad-
stone or the American Lincoln with nothing
but services to recommend him, would,
to-day, be passed contemptuously over by
Australian “society” in favour of one
whose father secured a place in Debrett by
means of a successful brewery, or a pushing
wife. Little wonder that Australian as-
pirations, as illustrated by the servility of
one section of her society, and the supine-
ness of another, should have become a by-
word-little wonder that the old taunt con-
cerning the weakness of Democrats for
decorations should find among us some
semblance of justification.
It would be uncharitable, however, to lay
too much emphasis on the fact that,* by
declaring themselves unfit to fill the post of
Governor-General, the colonists, through
their delegates have smudged their country’s
amour propre. They are but following the
example of their British kinsmen, who, for
over two hundred years, have elected to be
ruled by foreigners. Considering the tinus
and the conduct of the gloomy bigot
James, one almost forgives the Britons
of the 17th century’s closing decade for
making a clean sweep of the last Inal's
Stuart. At the same time the double-deal-
ing of the English Church on the occasion is
never likely to be forgotten. So long as the
bishops, those insolent followers of the
Socialist of Nazareth, were suffered to wring
their swollen incomes from half-starved
rustics, James might scourge and boot
British Nonconformists and Scottish
Covenantors to his heart’s content. It was
when His Majesty threatened to lay a heavy
hand on their lordships’ temporalities that
they discovered resistance to be lawful. But
what excuse can be made for inviting the
frowsy Georoes and the grotesque William
to rule a land that flaunted 'its squalid free-
dom in the world’s eyes 1—
George the First was reckoned vile—viler George the
Second,
And what mortal ever heard any good of George the
Third ?
When from earth the Fourth descended.
Heaven be praised, the Georoes ended.
Yet, for over six-score years these five
clowns—including “ Silly Billy ’’ with his
head like a pine-apple—their mistresses and
their brats had but to beckon in order to see
what was called the best society in the land
at their feet. To them appertained the
power of turning ribald priests into spiritual
lords—of conferring favours on many who in
these days would be felons. Small wonder
that the hankering after tinsel distinctions
should still find a place in the hearts of
Britons at home and abroad. It is doubtful
if any other nation in the world has stood
four generations of such monarchical.inepti-
tude. The colonists —we are assuming for
the sake of argument that the people
and the Convention are one in senti-
ment —are, therefore, not to be blamed
too harshly for their inability to emanci-
pate themselves at once from the tags and
tatters of the past. This refusal to elect
a Governor-General from among themselves
is but a survival in an abortive form of the
feeling that placed the Georges on the throne
and kept them there. Time alone will
remedy it. To the man of broad views like
Sir George Grey it is no doubt incompre-
hensible how men of ability far from mean
not only cling to the customs of by-gone
times, but, by a sort of mental tox-tuousness,
select some of the least defensible customs
to stand by. This surprise on Sir George’s
part will be all the greater when he reflects
that some of those who opposed his motion
were native Australians who thus by their
votes sat on the wrong end of the branch
while sawing it.
# # #
In this, as well as in many other respects,
the Americans had. a great advantage over
us Australians. Their blossoming into inde-
pendence was rapid, violent perhaps, but
salutary. At one wrench the whole was
done—King, Lords, Commons, titles, de-
corations, outside influence, and a dozen
other excrescences went overboard in one
splash. With us they are escaping furtively
through the scuppers while the State ship s
captain and officers swagger aimlessly on
deck protesting to the passengers that the
vessel is just as she left port, that nothing
has been jettisoned, and that she will return
to the British harbour exactly as when she
departed. She will never return. The
skipper and crew may swear to the contrary,
but the passengers now know almost as
much of navigation as the officers, and
will resent any attempt to “ ’bout
ship.” One thing alone the Austra
lian craft requires - an opportunity to
buffet the waves by herself for a time.
There is no fear of her going under, she is
too taut and staunch for that. Her officers
have certainly insisted on carrying a strange
captain, but that is because they themselves
have never had the sifpreme command of a
vessel, and dread the responsibilty. By and
by when they get more confidence, or the
passengers insist on sailing in a certain
direction, the commander will be requested
to step into the gig for removal to one of
his country's harbours in the vicinity. It
will not be necessary to throw him overboard
as a predecessor of his was thrown by a
kindred crew 115 years ago.
A few remarks remain to be made on
independent lands, and dependencies. Also
on the London Times, the Marquis of Lokne,
the Princess Louise, and Mr. Joseph,
P. Abbott, Speaker of the New South Wales
Assembly. To begin with the latter. At a
Convention meeting, he is reported to have
expressed his dissatisfaction with the
spread of the Republican sentiment in
New South Wales, and trusted some-
thing would be dune to stem it. From
an Australian-born person, who never
saw a monarch except an aboriginal one, the
foregoing is a cameo. On some politicians
the word “Republic” appears to have the
same effect as upon a British duke whose
coronet was manufactured in a trollop's
chamber. The fact is, nations which are not
independent ; which have no say in matters
of peace or war vitally affecting themselves ;
no diplomatic relations with foreign coun-
tries ; no authority to pass a divorce-law
suited to their circumstances without (he
consent of dainty adulterers thousands of
miles across the sea ; no power to annex a
rock a few leagues from their coasts, and no
competency to have the last word in an
important law-suit, may be prosperous and
even possessed of a certain smug c intent,
but no robust or virile public opinion can
secure a footing within their borders.
Without National Independence there will
always be timid men to lament the advance
of Republicanism and the decay of hereditary
misrule. It was good of the Times to rejoice
at the 1 of Sir G eoroe Grey’s motion.
It will console the majority of the delegates
to know they have on their side the journal
that screamed for war to permit the un-
•peakable Turk to work off the balance of
the Bulgarians, and that raked the kennels
on both sides of the Atlantic for evidence in
support of forgery. And now it is diplo-
matically spread abroad that the Marquis
of Lokne and the Princess Louise would
not mind coming to Australia as a sort of
dual Governor-General in the event of
Federation. This, if not the last straw, is
the last but one or two. In domestic matters
the change, no doubt, might be advantageous
to the young people, for the warmth of Aus-
tralia is credited with virtues not to be
expected in the snows of Canada, But in
politics, above all other pursuits, it is inju-
dicious to buy the skin of the bear while it
is still on him.
Judge and Jury.
We have no apology to offer for saying some-
thing more, or even repeating what we have
already said, upon the ludicrous inequality of
the sentences passed by different Judges on
criminals convicted of the same offence. If
there were no other side to the business than
the ludicrous one, we should expect our readers
to be tired of the joke, and drop it; but every
cloud has something to set off its silver lining,
and there is a serious side even to the comedy
that we humorously call the administration of
justice. It is funny at first sight to see Smith
sent up for five years for lifting a hundred
pounds, whije his brother-embezzler, Jones,
gets off with a six months’ stretch for £5OOO,
but it is rough on Smith, and it is bad for the
community. People of the British race like
fair play even for criminals, and they can’t
keep their respect for Courts that don’t give
fair play. The popular idea of punishment is
that it ought to lie proportioned to the crime,
and should not depend on the idiosyncracy of
the Judge. The Bulletin, seeing that it shares
that popular idea, claims that in a criminal
case the jury, and not the Judge, should pro-
nounce the sentence—that the men who are
entrusted with the duty of deciding upon the
fact of a prisoner’s guilt should also be allowed
tr decide the extent of his guilt.
* # #
It is no answer to say that the effect would
merely be to substitute the caprice of the jury
for the caprice of the Judge. The opinion of
one man is an individual opinion. The joint
opinion of twelve men chosen at random is a
more or less fair sample of the collective opinion
of the community ; and this collective opinion
ought to determine the amount of punishment,
within statutory limits, for a given crime. That
principle is already recognised in our Constitu-
tion. No Judge is given a free hand to ad-
minister punishment at his sole discretion;
Parliament lays down the limits within which
he is to act. For such an offence, he is told,
you can sentence the offender to not more than
ten years, or not less than three. That is to
say, the collective opinion of the community, as
expressed in Parliament, regards the various
grades of that offence as worthy of punishment
ranging between three and ten years. If there
were no grades recognised, there would be no
discretionary range of punishment. Now, when
the evidence in a particular case has been taken,
the grade of the offence is fixed. It is no
longer, for example, an abstract case of robbery
with violence, it is a concrete case of robbery
under such and such circumstances, and with
such and such a degree of violence. The com-
munity has expressed its collective opinion as to
the various degrees of punishment appropriate
to the various degrees of that crime ; and we
say that it is the collective opinion of the com-
munity that should allot the particular punish-
ment appropriate to that particular degree of
the crime. And, as the recognised medium
through which the community expresses its
collective opinion on crimes in the abstract
is Parliament, so the proper medium for the
expression of its collective opinion on crimes in
the concrete form is a jury.
So far as the logic of the question is concerned,
there is a good deal to be said in favour of revers-
ing the present position of affairs altogether,
and letting the Judge and jury change functions.
The judge is, or from his experience ought to
be, more skilled than the jury in weighing and
sifting evidence. Prima-face, perhaps, his
business should be to deliver the verdict, and
let the jury pronounce the sentence. That,
however, is outside the scope of our present
enquiry. We should not trouble about precise
lonic if the present system were practically
satisfactory and ensured substantial justice.
Before the affairs of men can be regulated with
the scientific precision of a delicate machine, the
motives that actuate men will have to be mastered
and measured and scheduled as minutely and as
thoroughly as the blind physical forces of
nature have been in the scientist’s laboratory.
That will take some time yet. Meanwhile we
ought to be, and we must be, satisfied with
rough justice and reasonable consistency in its
administration, but we don’t get it.
# * *
The ease stands this way. Let six Judges try
the same case on the same evidence, and they
will probably give six different—some of them
widely different—sentences, in accordance with
their varying sense of the moral gravity of the
crime. Six different juries, if fairly chosen,
would give more approximately the same sen-
tence, seeing that their average appreciation of
the quality of the offence would probably repre-
sent the average sense of the community. And
that average sense is the only true standard by
which the guilt of any offender can be measured.
Courts of law do not exist for the purpose of
enforcing any ideal morality. There are many
things that the moralist regards as criminal to
which the law keeps its eyes tightly shut. The
social-purity pietist we all know who, habitually
living at the rate of something more than a thou-
sand a year over his income, is systematically
robbing his creditors of that amount. He is
morally a thousand times worse, and he is a
thousand times more harmful to the community)
than the petty thief who robs a till or picks a


poeket J but tho law Avhich sends the petty
thief to gaol has nothing but obsequiousness for
Ifis white-waistcoated competitor in the whole-
sale line.
# # #
And quite properly so. The law is nothing
more, at any rate it has no business to be any-
thing more, than an authoritative expression of
public opinion; and public Opinion, unfor-
tunately, is not yet educated up to the pitch of
seeing any immorality in running up bills that
von know you can’t pay. No action, however
objectionable from an ethical point of view, that
does not meet Avith pretty general disapproval,
or pretence of disapproval, will ever get on to
the pages of the statute-book as a crime. (fur
national sensibility is jarred at the sight of a
carter flogging his horse, and cruelty to animals
is accordingly made a crime ; but avc do not, as
a nation, see any particular cause for unfavour-
able comment in the system that chains haggard
women and famished children to a hopeless life
of unending toil. So the sweater waxes fat and
flourishes, and the successful factory-master
grows to great honour in the State and is made
a G.M.G. Crime, in short, is that which
shocks the public sentiment, and it is only
because it does so that it is made punishable.
Consistency demands that in like manner the
degree of punishment should be measured by
the degree in which public sentiment is shocked ;
and that is only to be determined by the ex-
pression of public opinion through its authorised
mouthpiece, the jury.
* # *
There is nothing revolutionary in the proposal
to make the jury the judges not only of the fact
of criminality, but of its degree. In our civil
courts the principle is recognised to the full.
Had Reseigh Martin been sued in a libel
action by Commissioner Eddy, the jury, if they
found him guilty, would have had to assess the
penalty Avhich he had to pay, and in default of
payment he Avould have gone to gaol. Yet
that same jury, seeing that he was tried on the
criminal side instead of the civil side, Avere con-
sidered unfit to he entrusted with the assess-
ment of the penalty ! All they were allowed
to do was to find that as a fact he was guilty,
and the rest avos left to the Judge. Now, if a
jury is a fit tribunal to exercise the power of
punishing a man Avitli a fine of £IOOO or a
farthing for a libel or an assault, it is fit to
exercise the power of fixing the amount of his
punishment when the aggrieved individual seeks
satisfaction through the medium of the criminal
law. If it is not fit for the one, it is not fit for
the other. Even the criminal law as it stands
at present giA r es effect to the opinion of the jury
that a prisoner is not worthy of any punishment
at all, but it Avill calmly ignore their opinion
that he is worthy of 24 hours’ imprisonment. In
the first case the Judge is bound by the opinion
of the jury and must set the accused free, but
in the second case he is at liberty to treat them
Avith contempt and impose a penalty, if he
chooses, of 10 years’ penal servitude.
# #
Again, take the case of homicide. The jury
have three courses open to them. They may
acquit the prisoner, or, if they find him guilty,
they may bring in a verdict of either man-
slaughter or murder. In other words, they
have a choice between acquittal, imprisonment,
and death. What logical or reasonable objec-
tion could there be to extending the principle,
and, as they have the power. of distinguishing
between the degree of crime that deserves death
and the degree that is expiated by imprison-
ment, letting them distinguish between the
degrees that deserve longer or shorter terms of
imprisonment ? There would be no more practi-
cal difficulty inputting this system into operation
than is found in the existing system of civil
procedure ; in fact, it would merely he making
the practice uniform. The gist of a Judge’s
summing up in a civil case is this: “Gentle-
men, if you find that the plaintiff has not
proved his case, you must return a verdict for
the defendant. If, on the other hand, you find
that the plaintiff has proved his case, your ver-
dict must he for him, and it will then become
your duty to consider the question of damages.”
Then his Honor explains the principle on which
the jury are to assess the damages. What
difficulty would there lie in adapting the same
summing up to a criminal case ? “ Gentlemen,
if yon find the Crown Prosecutor has not proved
his case, return a verdict of not guilty. Rut if
you find the prisoner guilty—and you are sworn
to find your verdict as to the facts before giving
any consideration whatever as to the question
of punishment—then you must determine what
penalty he is to suffer. The law allows you to
award imprisonment for any term not exceeding
so many years, and not less than so many.”
And so on. That would he consistent, it Avould
he just, and it would be likely to give satisfac-
tion to the community. The present system is
glaringly inconsistent, grossly unjust, and if it
gives satisfaction to anybody The Bulletin
has not yet heard from him.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Gall of Monopoly.
There is an unfathomable reserve of cast-iron
cheek in the average human being. In, one of
Besawt’s novels, for example, the owner of an
asylum arrests a passing citizen under the im-
pression that he is an escaped lunatic, and when
he finds out his mistake, after he has chained
up his patient, and cut off his hair, and kicked
him, and straight-jacketed him, and emptied
forty buckets of water over him, he charges the
victim thirty guineas for a month’s board and
lodging in advance, and fires him out. This
proceeding, and especially the item, “wear
and tear of bucket, two guineas,” has always
occurred to us as a fair and reasonable example
of widespread cheek, but the South Australian
Employers’ Union has gone one better with
remarkable success. In a circular issued by
this gladsome institution the following para-
graph is conspicuous :
The time has now arrived when it is imperative that
capitalists and employers of labour should use the ballot-
box -
We pause in the middle of the sentence to
admire the sublimity of this idea. If is time
that capitalists should xtsi, the ballot-box 1 Tre-
mendous Moses ! The ballot-box is pretty
nearly Avorn to rags through the vigour Avitli
Avhicli they have used it already, and if they
use it any harder it will burst under the strain.
Doesn’t the capitalist wake up early every
polling-day, and go out and vote for his house
iii one electorate, and his club in another, and
his share in a syndicate in a third, and his
sheep-run and his six dummied selections in
seven more, and his Avild-cat gold-mine in some
other place ; and doesn’t he rout out all his
sons to do the same thing till there are forty■
nine votes in that one family? And then
doesn’t lie try to cajole or bounce "all his
employes to vote likewise, and spend the rest
of his leisure objecting to the working-man Avho
lias only one vote, and getting him struck off the
rolls ? And if he hasn’t been using the ballot-
box under all these varied circumstances, then
what on earth has he been using? We will
now consider the rest of the sentence :
—that is, if they wish to prevent the Labour party from
packing both Houses.
That strikes the summit, and the vast resources
of human gall could hardly go further. The
two Houses of the South Australian Parliament
are choke-full of landlords, squatters, mine-
owners, merchants, manufacturers, lawyers,
financiers, speculators, importers, and assorted
employers, and Labour, which forms nine-
tenths of the community, has hardly one
genuine representative. Labour .has done a lot
to return this gang of legislators, but it has
done it from sheer force of habit, for the work-
man has hardly begun to realise the possibility
of sending men of his oavh order into Parlia-
ment, so profound is his unconscious reverence
for a black coat, a large stomach, and a
quantity of jewellery. But when the workman
sends a capitalist into the Senate, that capitalist
represents him just as much as Louis Napoleon
represented the French Republic in the days
when the mighty liar was President in name,
and was preparing to be Emperor in reality.
The workman is beginning to realise this fact,
and it is his growing desire to be represented
by men of his own order, which the Employers’
Union describes as “packing both Houses.”
It would be interesting reading if that intelli-
gent body would supply answers to one or two
questions. First, if nine-tenths of the nation
has not a right to pack both Houses, then Avho
has that right ? Second, when one-tenth of the
nation—the tenth that merely represents capital
—fills the entire Legislature, what is the name
for the situation then existing —is it “pack-
ing?” and, if it isn’t, then what is it? Thirdly,
is Capital prepared to rise and openly move the
abolition of government by majority, and if it
isn’t, then does it propose to govern on the
assertion that it is the majority, and in that
case is there enough hard lying on earth to back
up the assertion ? The Employers’ Union can
find good material for another circular in fur-
nishing solutions for these three little problems.
Suicide.
Coroner Youl, of Melbourne, struck a large
deposit of common-sense the other day Avhen he
entered a protest against that time-honoured
verdict; “ Suicided while in a state of tempor-
ary insanity.” The verdict, no doubt, does no
particular harm to anybody, but it is eminently
ridiculous and a remnant of an objectionable
superstition, and if abstract truth is worth any-
thing, even when there is no monetary result
attached, such \ r erdicts ought to cease. For in
all reason and common intelligence there is no
evidence as to the state of anybody’s mind to
be deriv r ed from the mere fact that he committed
suicide; the act is one which can only be judged
by results, and no one really knows what the
results are. If this world be worth living in,
then the man who leaves it needlessly, and
before his time, is in a state of insanity, either
temporary or otherwise; if it be not worth
living in, then the man who suicides is a pro-
found philosopher and the lunatic is the man
who remains alive. Whether the present state
is good enough to stay in can only be decided,
however, by comparing it with the next one,
and the men who haA r e instituted that com-
parison are all dead; therefore, coroners and
jurymen and similar authorities, not being dead,
are unable to do more than indulge in vague
conjectures. In the old days the Church
had a habit of burying the suicide at
midnight at a spot where four roads met,
and driving a big stick through his dishonoured
carcase : and the Church at the present day
assumes that the suicide has gone to perdition ;
and, as it is unpleasant for any respectable
family to have a relative in perdition, the
average jury gets out of the difficulty by stating
that the deceased was not responsible for his
actions. Moreover, the jury is generally well-fed
and comparatively contented, and no man who has
just had a large dinner can readily understand
lioav any sane individual would face a risk of any
kind that he could possibly avoid. And yet if
the suicide was a Universalist in loav circum-
stances, Avho resided in a boiler and had no
worldly prospects whatever, or an Atheist who
camped out beneath a wharf and had no boots,
it is obvious that he did the most really sane and
reasonable act of his whole career Avhen he cut
that career short. A man who has nothing to
live for and something to gain by dying, might
lie a fair subject for an official inquiry if he
didn’t die, and there would bo abundant justifi-
cation for the A r erdict “ Remained alive owing
to temporary insanity,” but, so far as his death
is concerned, there is nothing to be surprised
about. And if he was a believer of the ordi-
nary type the verdict, in most cases, ought
to he : “ Died because this world Avas so
bad that he didn’t think the next could be any
worse”—or, in other words, “ Died because this
world Avas a horrible certainty and the next was
only an unpleasant conjecture,” The average
suicide, in short, dies through sheer poverty.
He has to choose between suiciding and robbing
somebody. If he suicides the law is considerate
when consideration is no good to him, and says
he Avas in an irresponsible state ; whereas if he
robs somebody he gets no consideration at all,
but goes to gaol. And, being sane, and there-
fore able to appreciate the dilemma, he takes
the only way out of it with a bit of borroAvcd
rope, Avhereas if he Avere a lunatic he would pro-
bably fail to realise the full measure of-the
difficulty, and go on living. Taking it all
round, the twelve thickheaded men who sit
on the suicide’s remains might as Avell cease
to make unfounded assertions about, the intelli-
gence of the dead. When they find that the
deceased is a corpse they are on safe ground,
but when they go beyond that they are fooling
with the mysteries of the unknowable, and
when eight butchers and four whitewashes and
house-painters undertake to explain the con-
struction of the human intellect, they have a
large contract on hand.

The Many Cities of Melbourne.
Footsokav, the muddiest and most evil-smell-
ing of Melbourne suburbs, has just assumed the
meaningless title of “city,” and his Worship
the Mayor is expanding his chest in honour of
the fact that alone he did it. There are now
nine cities included in the capital of Victoria.
Very soon the number will he SAvelled, by civic
asininity, to nineteen, and the time is not far
distant when ninety inflamed persons in fur-
trimmed skirts will each haA r e a city to his own
puffy cheek. The beAvildered resident of Mel-
bourne is troubled Avith a new sort of Athana-
sian Creed, to the effect that he lives in nine
cities and not one-city—without reckoning the
towns about to become cities or the boroughs on
the verge of breaking out into towns. The
evolution of Melbourne boroughs into cities is a
simple and contemptible process. When a
sufficient number of jerry-built imitations of the
English bathing-machine have been slung to-
gether in the centre of a swamp, the residents
are secretly incited by somebody to form
themselves into a borough. Whereupon
an enterprising contractor runs up another
acre or so of populous neighbourhood on
time-payment and adds a couple of two-storey
brick pubs in commanding positions. After
which the population adjourns to the pub. that
sells most beer for threepence, at the invitation
of a person aa lio treats the crowd and declares
that the expanding borough of Swampslush
must assert its independence by establishing a
Town Hall, and electing him to the position of
Mayor. More drinks are ordered in, and the
resolution is carried unanimously. In due
course the toAvn is “produced,” a Toavii Hall
rears its head like a pelican in the wilderness,
and a little Municipal slioav is run by a com-
pany of barnstormers until the Mayor of the
hour (perhaps the same one who “ shouted ” in
the first instance) decides that the eye of the
world has cast a significant glance at .Swamp-
slush, by way of a reminder that it ought to
assume t he dignity of ‘ ! city. ” By this time there
may be, as in the Footscray case, some six
thousand ratepayers on the roll, and the craving
of a man, whom most of them have never heard
of, for a distinction which doesn’t make two-
penn’orth of difference to anybody else is
treated Avith apathetic scorn. So the Mayor
and corporation invite their beloved Governor
to come along in his yelloAV barouche, and on
the appointed day Houetoux makes triumphal
entry under four strings of flags, and informs
the assembled multitude of sixty-seven souls
that he has much pleasure in proclaiming
Swampslush a city, because it was gazetted a
city the other day, and he can see loyalty to
the CroAvn written on every brick. This busi-
ness being disposed of, the Mayor conducts his
Governor to luncheon, and everybody drinks
the health of the Queen, and his Excellency,
and the new City, with great fury. There the
matter ends. Another ill-drained district has
allowed another lump of human vanity to
glorify himself in its name, and, if he' pays for
the luncheon, all well and good. If the luncheon
is consumed out of the rates, however, then this
childish changing of a sham town into a bogus
city is merely a scandalous occasion for another
dirty little job. Anyway, Victoria will soon
require a special Act of Parliament to restrain
the pomposity of its thousand and one metro-
politan magnates. Melbourne itself can never
lie more than a city, yet within its boundaries
there will soon be included a feAV gross of
Dominions, and a good round dozen Empires,
unless the Law prohibits mayors from “pro-
claiming ” themselves egregious asses any
further.

Home Rule for the A.M.P. Society.
Next month the animal election of directors
for the A.M.P. Society, the great and truly
Federal institution of which not only Ncav
South Wales but the whole of Australasia is
proud, Avill be held in Sydney. For two
vacancies on the directorate, no feAver than
twelve men of high professional or mercantile
standing have been nominated. A new point
to be suggested to the annual meeting is the
desirability of amending the Society’s rules, so
that each province shall elect its own board of
directors. This reform carries with it, on its
face, the reasons for its acceptance. At present,
the local boards are the appointments of the
central office, but the policy is strongly to be
reprehended notwithstanding it has been of
service in the past—on the ground that it
must lead, with the expanding business of the
passing years, to centralisation with all its in-
evitable abuses. The system of electing directors
by the members to the local directorates would
he simply an extension of the mutual principle.
The more distributed the government, whether
it be of a society or a political body, the
stronger the feeling of mutual dependence and
of reciprocal interests. It is the mutuality of
the A.M.P. which has been the loadstone of its
phenomenal success, and it needs no argument
to demonstrate that the greater the share of
power retained by the policy-holders, the more
direct their interest in the society’s prosperity,
and in the maintenance of its prestige. The
election of local men would make the relation of
a policy-holder closer and more personal to the
association, for where one has a personal know-
ledge of the elective directors noAv, a thousand
Avould have such under the projected plan.
Again, nominee appointments to the local board
might (Ave do not say they have been so in the
past) prove inimical to the society’s prosper! ty,
because the local board being a nominee one,
could hardly, upon any broad question of policy,
conflict with their nominators. For these
reasons, The Bulletin intends to plump Avhen
the time arrives for a decentralisation of direc-
torial control. And Avhile alluding to Iho
Society's affairs we may throAV out the idea that,
now that the safe policy of high premiums has
been so brilliantly demonstrated, the Associa-
tion might consider the advisability of reducing
its premium rates. It will have before long to
'meet the problem of declining returns upon
investments ; it cannot expect to yield bonuses
of equal value to those which have accrued in
the past in the face of a declining percentage of
interest ; and large surpluses are by no means
the first thing to be considered when there is a
difficulty in the selection of investments. Now,
a lowering of the premium means an immediate
bonus to the policy-holder without the trouble
and expense of, first of all, collecting and then
returning the bonus. We are quite aware thai
a big bonus is a capital advertising card, but a
society of the stability, the scope, and the pres-
tige of the A.M.P. can surely, at this epoch of
its history, disdain and cast away every vestige
of the tricks of the canvasser.
Christian Torture.
It is the fashion of those smug and self-com-
placent persons who pose as the Teachers of the
People, to prate about the superior enlighten-
ment and humanity of present days. We are
told, ad nauseam, that every man nowadays,
however humble and insignificant, is sure of
justice and fair play in his treatment by our
constituted authorities, and that it is the pride
and praise of modern times that tyranny and
cruelty and all that sort of thing are dead and
buried. Yet we sometimes find that notwith-
standing these assurances, things are done
which could scarcely have been outdone in the days
when the rack, the thumbscrew, and such-like
agents of the law, were recognised and approved
institutions. A case in Melbourne illustrates
this. A Hindoo named Fatta Chand was
charged, some three months ago, with the
murder of a fellow black, of the name of
Juggoo Mull. The jury disagreed on
the first trial, and the prisoner, after
lying a further period of a month in
gaol, was tried again. This time the
Crown, as represented by the Crown Prosecutor
and the Judge, took care that there should he
no disagreement. Fatta Ctiand was found
guilty, and cast for death. Well and good, so
far; since, although the evidence against the
accused was purely circumstantial, there was
no moral doubt of his guilt. But here comes in
the wrong which was done to the man. He
was placed in a “condemned coll,” loaded with
the usual amount of irons, and kept there a
■ month in suspense as to his fate, because the
Governor was away in the malice country,
and Premier Munro was in Sydney help-
ing to fix up the Federation scaffolding.
No Executive could be got together until
Fatta Chand, who refused to eat, had lan-
guished a full month after being sentenced to
death. When his case wm considered
and the death-warrant was signed, the date of
execution Avas fixed for a da fortnight
thence. In answer to the
Minister of Justice, Mr. Davies,, with sickening
cant, said that it was desirable to keep Fatta
Ck.vxb alive for such further time, in order
that he might have an opportunity of embracing
the Christian faith, and learning the art of
getting full salvation from his sins. So the
miserable man Avas kept on and, still refusing to
eat, was fed artificially. Three times a day lie
Avas thrown upon his back, strapped doAvn, and
a certain quantity of nourishment pumped into
his stomach. This was done by means of an
ingenious contrivance by Avhich obstinate sub-
jects, closing their teeth, are fed through
a long pipe passed through a nostril
down the throat, and so into the epigastrium of
the victim. This Avas done in order to keep up
Fatta Chand’s strength and spirits during the
period allotted by the Minister of Justice for
his conversion to Christianity. If he suffered
bodily, it Avas that his soul might be saved.
And that this desirable end might be achieved
an ancient missionary, who spoke Hindustanee,
took a hand in the game. Twice each day he
attended the condemned man and attempted to
pump the water of life into his poor intellectual
system. Thus the man Avas fed up for the
gallows, instead of being hanged right away, in
order that he might have a chance of learning
how the Feringhis send their murderers straight
from the hangman’s grip to the bosom of Jesus.
It Avas labour lost, hoAvever. Fatta Chand
took no more kindly to Christian consolation
than to Dr. Shield’s injected beef-tea. He
Avent to the galloAVS, no doubt, with a proper
contempt for his tyrants, and glad enough to
escape them. He died a veritable martyr, and,
to our mind, illustrating a circumstance we
have frequently pointed out—to Avit, that our
civilisation is but veneer, and that for all our
boasted nineteenth-century sAveetness and light
avc are capable of as brutal outrage as any
breech-clouted savage dancing round a pro-
spective “ long pig,” or any agent of the Inqui-
sition at work with the Devil’s tools in the
name of God and true religion.

The Argus tells us that—
The loss on the withdrawal of old silver coin and all
expenses in connection with the issue of new coin have
been defrayed solely by the Imperial Government, no
charge having been made to either the local Govern-
ment or the public.
This would lead to the impression that people
are under an obligation to the Imperial GoA r ern
ment for meeting losses and defraying expenses
in this connection; whereas exactly the reA T erse
is the case. An enormous profit accrues at all
times to the Imperial Government from the
coinage of silver, for the silver coin of Britain is
a mere token having a legalised and not an
intrinsic value. The illicit coiner has no induce-
ment to waste his “art” on our silver money
(that is in producing low-grade and spurious
tokens) as the profit from the legitimate material
yields him sufficient recompense for his labours.
Witness the fact that a firm of German coiners,
some years ago, actually put half-crown and
two shilling pieces—manufactured in Germany
—into circulation in England, and although
“spurious” in laAv, because being from other
than the Imperial Mint, they were, as a matter
of fact, better in quality and of greater intrinsic
value. How about thh fluctuating Values of the
subsidiary coinages of India and Hong Kong ?
They come from the Imperial Mini, but the cir-
culating dependencies having ini gold standard,
their values arc regulated by that of bar silver.
Why lidk of loss and place ns under ohliga
linns ? •

kid Macquarie-slreet North, Sydney, April 29, 1891,
Mr Dear Moorabinda,—
Sydney Horticultural Society’s ’santhemum display at
the local Town Hall on 22nd ult. was by far the most
successful show, in every way, that this vigorous Society
has ever held. There were loads of fine specimens of the
Japanese flower (though lam noadmirerof Brobdingnagian
blooms ot dropsical development forced beyond the design
of Nature into floral monstrosities), one root bearing
flowers in crimson, yellow, white and old gold ; the Un-
believing present got down on all-fours to see that the
phenomenon wasn’t managed with a heap of gardener’s
guile and a little string. The ’mums were the piice de
resistance, but all the greenery available at present was
on view, and one little side-show of onions and carrots
and so on, was the centre of the adoring crowd which
always lingers at the shrine of something filling. The
decorated tables were mostly pretty, and I rejoiced to see
that the recent mania for stacks of verdure has exhausted
itself, and more moderate and possible floriture adopted
—exhibit 181 being nicest in my eyes, a fairy-like
mingling of white and scarlet and ferns, with a glass
lake, in the centre of which two water-lilies were “making
believe ” to float, its delicate taste being’ enhanced by a
vulgar arrangement across the way in all the colours of a
Chinese lantern.
A regular crash—headed by Lord and Lady Jersey, who
opened the show—turned up on two-and-sixpenny day
and promenaded to the strains of Vittorini’s band, and
there were one or two frocks. Lady Jersey (now wasn’t
it very odd that there was no committee bouquet at such
a proceeding?) was sketchy in limp cream and yellow
striped silk trimmed with gold and silver guipure, bonnet
in cream straw, cream feathers and pink flowers, pink
fan ; Mrs. W. P. Manning, Sydney’s new Mayoress (a tall
matronly Mayoress), in grey with trimmings of slate-
colour silk, bonnet en suite ; Miss Manning, in dark-blue
and a black hat; Mrs. Havilland Reynolds (wife of the
hon. see.) in aubergine serge, black hat with aubergine
plumes, did the show with Lord Jersey in a turquoise tie;
the Misses Halliday in workman-like dresses of black
serge braided in black, black hats relieved respeotively
with tomato-red and canary-yellow velvet; Mrs.
Vauderveer Green in leaf-brown, toque en suite.
In her opening speech Lady Jersey spoke of the
“ beautiful fields of Australia,” and thereby emphasised
the fact that she is still a new-chum who probably thinks
of the bush as “ the woods.” I once heard a lisping new-
chum speak of the Australian “meadovvth” to an old
pioneer. The ancient regarded him with a baleful glare
and then contemptuously replied: “D’ye mean to tell
me, Mister, that you dunno a (expletive) paddick when,
yer see one ?”
The Metropolitan LiedertafeTs moonlit-concert at the
Association Ground, on the 24th ult., was a novel sort of
ceremony, which would have been highly successful had
it not ended in driving rain, and if the lighting on the
grand-stand had not been left to strings of Chinese
lanterns, which went out or caught light in their old,
sweet way, while people had to strike matches to see
what was the next item on the card: Of the vocal efforts
of the warblers (who occupied a platform in the centre
of the lawn and were assisted by a regimental brass
band), the Pilgrim’s chorus from “ Tannhauser " was the
most artistic item. Abt’s “ Evening ” (with Mr. Clancy
singing the solo part in rather a robust manner for such
tender melody) gained a furious recall, but the staccato
passages in “ The Frontier Line ” were so clumsily done
that an audible grin went up from the crowd—number-
ing thousands of people. There was absolutely no
or three misguided creatures, who turned
out in white evening frocks and clouds, looking very eerie
in the cold moonlight; while one woman wore a plush
wrap over a snowy decollete bosom in the teeth of the
creepy blast. Ido hope she had the sense to rub her nose
with a tallow-candle when she got home.
And apropos to the concert, some people gush over ic
as a “delightful innovation in this climate” which
“ought to become general,” but, for my part, unless
things are bettor managed, I would prefer music in a
concert-room, where the acoustics are good and the air is
at least free from tobacco. The other evening every man
on the lawn was smoking a different compound, and when
you got a whiff of them altogether—ugh! Besides which.,
moonlight concerts seem to be as spoony as moonlight
picnics. In every corner the golden head of Angelina
leant stiffly against the coat-collar of her Edwin, and one
or tw’o souls but with a single thought did the lawn (when
the moon slipped under a cloud) with “his’’arm about
“her’’waist! Indeed, one felt as if one had intruded
unwittingly at a honeymoon resort.
Speaking of honeymoons, there is a growing tendency-
on the part of newly-married couples to keep their place-
of refuge a close secret, and the unbelieving among the'
wedding-guests say that this mystery is resorted to when
the culprits are only going to moon about the next suburb.,
so to speak. Certainly when Mount Victoria or Tasmania:
is the destination everybody seems to know it, but when;
“secresy” is observed it’s a hundred to one you cannon
off the recent connubialities on the beach at Coogee.

stances that your blushing Sappho cannot even hint at.

Newington College complimentary sports attracted a
3000 attendance to the Association Ground on Wednesday
week, when tho usual array of beauty tripped up and
down the lawn utterly regardless of the sports themselves,
as usual. From the frocking on view one gathered the
impression that the world is hanging fire with its autumnal
outer covering, and consequently if it hadn’t been for a
slight, erect damsel in grey cheviot, made with a New-
market coat with the collars faced with grey silk,
Toreador “ shapo ” in grey felt with a bob at the back of
the crown of grey and cardinal pom-poms, even the
ladies’ reserve would have looked like an airing-ground
for resurrected raiment. The performers wore the usual
whirl of prismatic guernseys over inverted-saucer chests,
and wrinkled hosen over axe-handle shanks, and the
band played all the time. P. Kelynack won the principal
trophy, and is now chief athlete of the college.

chrome-green faced with bars of scarlet, lavender and
white scarf ijpund the waist and white jaconet shirt. As

“ They ” are wearing “ their ”_hair bird’s-nest fashion in
Melbourne just now, and the swagger Victorienne is
popularly supposed to complete her coiffure by rushing

bonnet on. In Sydney the fashionable heads are getting

A VVaverley (N.S.W.) domestic was left a legacy of
£12,000 the other day. Her mistress, on receiving the

with, “ I am glad to hear of your good fortune, Bridget.’’
At which all the fiery scorn of an heiress flashed into tho
boiled eye of the hireling—“ If you plaze, Mrs. Blank, me
name is Miss O’Hooligan,”
At a recent meeting of a Melbourne Ladies’ Debating
Club the question whether “ Men or Books ” had most
influence on the character was decided by a large
majority in favour of the former ! Indeed, one girly grad,
read a very eloquent paper to prove that men had more
influence in the formation of character than books 1! The
cultured, spectacled ’Varsity girl is only a skin-deep
pedant after all, ready to drop philosophy and run a fire,
side whenever she gets a chance.
From Miss A. M. Summerbelle, of Double Bay, I have
received three musical compositions—two of which, the
“Myee Waltz ” and “ Rondo in G ” (ii Maurice), published
some time ago, have not the charm of novelty which the
recently-issued “Gavotte in D. Major” possesses. The
gavotte ought to take, for the first movement in scattered
chords is very tuneful, and as it contains no technical
difficulties, seems to be the very piece for advanced
beginners. It is sold at all music-shops. Miss Summer-
belle will shortly publish a baritone song, written by
request for Mens. Deslouis.
Sydney thought it had been struck by a new kind of
maritime circus the other day when a brougham drawn
by a crowd of tars with two on the box dashed through
the streets to the railway-station, with the crowd whoop-
ing them on their way and a choice assortment of gutter-
snipes swarming in the wake. But it was only a naval
officer and his bride being sent off with alleged nautical
honours, and the spectacle was enough to make any girl
take a solemn vow that if she was ever inveigled into
matrimony with a maritime person she would escape
immediately afterwards in a balloon. I never saw two
people look more supremely idiotic in my life. I caught.a
fleeting glimpse of the panorama as it shinned ahead, and
—well, I did sympathise with the poor blushing victims
inside.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.

Mr. John Lawler, senr., of the firm of Lawler and Sons,
George-street, Sydney, who left for Europe on Monday
last, received a cordial send-off at the hands of ninety of
the firm’s employees on the previous Saturday at his
Glebe Point residence, when they presented to him an
illuminated address. At the banquet which followed, the
gentleman who proposed Mr. Lawler’s health alluded to
him as one of the fairest employers of labour in Australia,
and stated that if there were many such as he, there
would be few, if any, strikes. This statement having been
warmly endorsed by several of the “ hands,” the evening
was otherwise filled by the rendering of many excellent
vocal and instrumental items.

“ The Corsican Brothers,” Boucicdiilt’s
version of Grange and Montepin’s adaptation
from Dumas pore’s story, was last played in
Sydney by Alfred Dampier, at the Standard in
July, ’B7. Well-staged, and well-handled, all
round, it is now likely to catch on at Her
Majesty's, On Saturday night the first act with
its “ hen-scene,” well played by Messrs. Harcourt
and Tolano, an artistically-devised village dance,
and a fine ghost scene, went with a bang. Here,
Fabian dei Franche, inlighting Mine, de Franchei
to tho door makes a clever “ prompt ” exit, Jack
Sweeney takes his place at the writing-table, back
to audience and the ghost of Lotus dei Franchi,
aided by the “Corsican trap ” comes up from the
hearth and gradually emerging into view, crosses
the stage obliquely, finally disappearing in the
clock. In the second act (by the way, if this were
made the first it would rid the now first of much
cumbersome explanation) a tableau curtain shut
off half the Opera House ball-scene and here
(on first night only) an attempt to attain
realism by breaking into speeches with gusts
of laughter from the dancers, spoiled several
front scenes and destroyed continuity. Then
the gallery chipped in and refused to be com-
forted till dei Franchi bellowed at them, “Silence,
you beasts, silence!” The third tier in this house
is very highly pitched, and when it is crowded and
the proscenium border not well raised —well, then,
those at the rear, failing to see the back of the
stage, are apt to amuse themselves by preventing
those who can see from hearing. On first night
the mirroi’ effects were not so successful as usual,
owing to the fact that the apparatus was fixed at
the wrong angle, but this has since been remedied.
Tho dei Franchi of George Rignold, well-garbed
and “ made-up,” is a good and faithful perform-
ance, whose chief fan t, we think, is delibera-
tion of utterance. To our mind, the denuncia-
tion of Chateau Renaud in the forest should
not be spoken like the unimpassioned indictment
qt a judge, but should pour forth with the
impetuosity of a suddenly-liberated torrent, or be
relentlessly hissed out, so as to give the idea of
repressed passion, just as steam forced from a
crater betrays the' intense heat of the volcano
within. The Chateau Renaud of Harry Jewett is
good in conception and result, especially in the
Fontainbleau scene, where his impersonation of the
manner in which the implacable, immovable
swordsman is weakened by superstitious dread, is
masterly. It is not often a stage duel between
men of such physique is witnessed, and its only
defect is over length The Emilie de Lesparre of
E lith Blande seems to sit on her more easily than
anything she has done of late. Arthur Greenaway,
emaciated by illness, and Harry Hoyte just re-
turned from Melbourne, made welcome reappear-
ances as Martelli and Mongiron. We don’t know
why the last act is shutoff fiom the audience by a
gauze. If it be, as reported, to suggest a hazy
atmosphere, then it is absurd, for frost clears the
air.

“ Modern Wives,” an adaptation by
Erne-t Warren from “La Bonheur Conjugal,”
succeeded “Tho Colonel”—he might have easily
lived through another week —at Sydney Criterion,
on Saturday. “Modern Wives,” last played
here in August, ’BB, is a bright and unpretentious
little play that is full of good lines and situations,
though much more might have been made by the
adaptor of the complications worked up to in the
last act. Brough, as the retired hatter, whose
married daughters have got divorce badly (it
comes like the influenza from Chicago, probably),
and who is about to give £5OOO as dowry to his
little girl, as an offset to her piano-playing, is in
his element, and his Caleb Chubb is without spot
or blemish. Cecil Ward has the next best show,
and his Noel Goldring is bright, lively and
consistent. Emma Temple, as Agatha, the young
shrew, whose husband states that she was altered
at the altar, if not changed at birth, was scarcely
hard and bitter enough. Arthur Lawrence, as
Honeysett, the soft hearted deserted man, has not
an enviable part, but he handles it rightly, and
the cut of his “clobber he makes three changes
—was much admired by a discriminative “circle.”
As Grace Goldring, the wife who is a perpetual
grace before cold meat, Mrs. Brough has a very
small part to handle. G. L. Gordon and JNellie
Mortyne are similarly blessed or cursed, just as
they chose to look at things. The young lady
whose sisters unhappiness does not preclude her
from leaping into matrimony, is not over well
played by Brenda Gibson, but her love-scene with
R. Harris, an idiotic doctor of the Webster in
“ Dr. Bill ” stamp, was much appreciated by
unfledged mashers and bread-and-butter misses
the other evening. “ Modern Wives ” has caught
on, but the management, resolved to take no
chances, put up “Imprudence,” one of Pinero's
earliest, first produced at London Folly in ’Bl, on
Saturday first.

At Sydney Garrick stage-room is now
filled by Johnny Sheridan’s version of “Little
Black-Eyed Susan,” an olio of absurdities more
remarkable for the opportunities afforded for the
introduction of songs and dances than for the
faithfulness with which it burlesques the original
story. The great Sheridan plays the smuggler
who afterwards assumes command of a warship,
and finally becomes a judge, with a vast amount of
humour and go, and although he has never in his
life tasted intoxicants, makes a big hit
through the verisimilitude of his drunken
scene. Alfred James fitly plays tho Widow
Hatley, a bibulous, battered old party whose
personification must have cost the gifted
Alfred hours of police-court study. Gracio White-
ford tackles Susan, the cause of all the trouble,
and achieves, as usual, success. The William of
Stella Tudor is au elegant being, who has a dandy
American accent and a neatly-turned ankle.
Imano is the Doggrass, and his true, round voice is
heard to advantage throughout. The Snow ballet
is just a bit cold to look at these chilly nights, but
is otherwise lovely, and the Sailors’ ballet is
sweeter than jam. “ Fun on the Britol ” goes up
on Saturday night.

Friday nights with tho Divine Williams
are a marked success at Melbourne Alexandra,
where the Bard came under the patronage of Lord
Hopetoun (and suite) last week. Consequently,
“ As You Like It” will be played yet a third
time to-morrow (Ist), and would run on for
several more Fridays, but that the extensive
character of Dampier’s approaching production
will probably necessitate the Bard’s retirement for
a time. Miss Lily Dampier’s performance of
Rosalind is a pretty, poetical piece of work, at
once light and legitimate. Good in the first act,
she subsequently plays the boy in the best of
styles, wearing her doublet and hose with as much
ease as modesty, and never calling attention to
her sex by over-acting the part of the other fellow.
Coming after her long sequence of persecuted
wives and weeping mothers, this cheerful comedy
suggests the sunshine that follows the rain, or the
rainbow in the clouds. As Swinburne might
observe, if he were versed in Australian affairs—
Dampier’s fine voice and practised elocution are
as comforting as ever in the part of the moralising
Jaques, whilst Appleton’s Orlando is tolerably
young in appearance, if not in manner. But he
has a trick, unpardonable in such a well-seasoned
actor, of pausing and dropping his voice at the
end of every line. Robert Vernon’s rendering of
old father Adam is well spoken and sound; and,
of the others, Mrs. Dampier as Celia, and J. H,
Martin as Touchstone, are about the best. Several
minor characters, however, betray evidences of
the boss’s experienced coaching, and the fact that
sundry members of the company have respectable
singing voices makes itself heard in the course of a
rather musical evening. Finally, “As You Like
It ” is mounted with more respect for the feelings
of the late William S. than they sometimes receive,
under similar conditions, at higher-priced houses.

“The Lyons Courier,” which seems
nearly identical with the “Mail” that Irving
runs, filled the Alexandra bill on Saturday night.
As the virtuous Joseph Lesurques, alternately
with the bold, bad, brandy-proof Dubose, his
shadow, Dampier holds this classic melodrama in
either hand, and is equally clever with both. A
little more bustle and an infusion of husky whis-
per might improve the mail-robbery scene, while
a faster tongue throughout would better suit
Dubose’s business than the rounded utterances
which make him appear, morally as well as
physically, like an upright Joseph on the criminal
burst. Eacli character is clean cut, however, the
figure of Dubose being appallingly picturesque at
times. The stage fate of this gory ruffian is not
according to French records, but it preaches a
beautiful temperance sermon, anyway. If he
hadn’t kept a bottle to his lips at the critical
moment he might have gloated over the decapita-
tion of his “double,” and have lived to enjoy a
respectable funeral. As it is, he gets puled to
pieces all along of the accursed drink, which is
historically incorrect but otherwise very right
and proper. The robber band played rather out
of tune on Saturday, and Lesurques pere was
about as pathetic as a bull with a bad cold,
but seeing that the play's the thing and the main
thing is Dampier it is to be expected that “The
Lyons Courier ” will flourish until the coming
sensation is fixed up. And this same novelty
threatens to send Melbourne into fits. The entire
Wild West Show will be drafted into the new
Dampier drama, and Dr. Carver, his Injuns, and
cowboys will assist the plot by falling off bridges
on horseback, and putting bullets through the
leading villain after he has been scalped and
lassooed. May Bth is the date fixed for opening
this show of compound interest.
Melbourne’s dazzling halls of song and
dance are still hard at it, likewise the Waxworks
kangaroo continues to pat his adversary in an
aimless manner. Meanwhile Carver’s “Wild
America” will keep on at the Societies’ Gardens
until Alexandra arrangements are completed. .

The fame of “The Old Guard,” at Mel-
bourne Princess’, grows as rapidly as mustard and
cress, or bad habits, and people are nightly
turned away from the doors like mendicants who
ask church elders for sixpence when the world
isn’t looking, and no newspaper is ready to publish
a list of subscriptions. The secret of this boom is
still vested in Elton and the ballerinas, of whom
the front girl in yellow is a delightful dish ot
curry in herself. There are plenty of respectable
citizens who would gladly sit and watch such leg*
ascending by the hour together—-not that ky*
ever ascend together, because the Commonwealth
wouldn’t be strong enough to stand it if they did.
Paraphrasing an immortal verselet by Sir Ben-
jamin Backbite — _ - ;
Sure, never was seen such sublime ballerinas:
All fairies are fillies, but these fillipinas.
To give them this title is senseless and wrong—
Their kicks are so high and their skirts are so long-
Those languors of Lily’s we pardon
When dramas condemn her to gloom—
’Tis here, in the Forest of Arden,
We find the fair blossom in bloom.
The heart of the chappie she captures,
His spiiit is held in a vice;
When set off by roses and raptures
The Lily’s uncommonly nice.

George Darrell, at the Royal, was much
neglected during the larger part of last week, but
on Saturday there was a good attendance, pro-
bably swollen by the closing of the Opera House,
to welcome the return of that noble hero to
“The Sunny South.” George is ever at his
greatest in the character of Mat Morley. His
calm defiance of the conventionalities of life is a
profound study, and his top-boots form a splendid
index to bis character. Babs Berkeley, also, is
another tremendous opportunity for Mrs. Darrell,
and the remaining people scramble for whatever
opportunities are left, with considerable cleverness
in some cares, and precious little in others. Not
the least entertaining feature of a Darrellian
drama is the playbill, constructed on pantomime
principles. To avoid the possibility of a mistake,
George labels his various creations. Thus the
heroine is sometimes introduced to notice as
“a true woman,” so that her sex can be perceived
at a glance, or an aged chunk of the opposite per-
suasion appears in the well-defined capacity of
“ the old, old story,” lest he might attempt to
pass himself off as a young, young anecdote
or something. In “Sunny South ” we get some
touching illustrations of George’s desire to make
things easy for his patrons. The merest child
understands that “ Ivo Came (of the genius new-
chum) ” must be a gifted recent arrival, and there’s
no getting away from “ a representative digger.”
He can’t be a shipping-clerk out of a billet, or the
foreman of a boot-factory. Then there’s “Bank-
teller True (a brave example).” You are prepared
for True all the time, and rejoice to think that in
the hour of peril that estimable cashier is doomed
to do his dooty. But one waits in vain for the
programme whereon George himself will appear
as “ villain of the deepest dye,” or, better still, as
“excited populace (with muffled groans in the
distance).” Perhaps “Sunny South ” will hang
on until the talented Cartwright-Nethersole Com-
pany return to the rescue.
Several hundred babies will be divided off
in pens at Melbourne Exhibition during the four
last days of this week. Arrangements are of a
more luxurious character than last time, and the
infants should be in full blast when this notice
appears.

“Evangeline” eventuated at Melbourne
Opera House on Monday evening, but no per-
ceptible reason for importing this slow paralysis
of the brain from America can be divulged by
telegraph. The primeval burlesque that moulders
in an unhallowed grave was an intellectual feast
as compared with “ Evangeline,” and the average
pantomime rejects the Yankee notion with con-
tumely —a libretto of incoherent puerility, as inter-
mingled with songs, set to music, of that simple
reminiscent character which men compose
whilst washing themselves, and chortle out
from amidst the suds, the result being
a withering blast to modern civilisation and a
shock to the Darwinian theory. Even more inex-
plicable than the bringing of this mildewed
Mosaic work across tiie ocean was the circumstance
of four alleged American damsels coming with it.
Three of them are mere lightly clad nonentities,
whilst the fourth, a Miss Earl, is a star such as
may be gathered by the bushel from the London
Music-hall stage. Two male performers have also
arrived with their original parts. Fortescue, a
carefully - wigged and painted mountain of
flesh, figures in the decollete capacity of
Aunt Catherine, and narrowly escaped being
a degrading spectacle in misapplied mil-
linery. Some evidence of worthy talent may
presently ooze from Fortescue’s ton of adipose
tissue, but he did not perspire that way on Mon-
day. Even for caravan purposes this exaggerated
stranger falls about six stone short of Sir Roger
in his palmy days. A lone fisherman, described
as a household word in America, likewise pervades
the gaudy misery, contributing streaks of harm-
less dumb pantomime and one eccentric dance.
-Vs a rule, however, the lone fisherman is a deep-
lyed nuisance, sitting on a camp-stool, and his
inaudible pretentiousness is the most charac-
teristic feature of a bombastic production,
lor the rest, Fanny Liddiard graces the
feeble name part, and Gourlay, whose principal
employment is propping up the skirted monster,
manfully under his burden. Snazelle,
rurde and the stock company pop up fitfully
when the stage is not occupied by a pantomime
Wv.and other inane apparitions. The mounting
of ‘Evangeline” is effective, although discounted
y front scenes, whilst the dressing is rich and
elegant, but these accessories will scarcely mollify
audiences educated up to a still higher standard
of splendour elsewhere.
* * * at
Sprue affable American person proposes to
avect a Bunker’s Hill cyclorama in Melbourne, and
ffie tacts concerning that famous battle will be
only laid before the Victorian public. As wo
nave already been bored to the last stage of ex-
haustion by excited Yankees who talked with a
ucleous iteration about that awful military con-
-fisston, and as we have been harassed a lot by
■ ook-canvassers, and sewing-machine men, and
agents for celluloid teeth, and individuals who
travelled selling hair-restorer in a bottle, and
parties who traded in imperishable bone cuffs, and
persons who dealt in the indestructible bird-of-
treedom shirt that goes out in the dead of night,
and secretly washes itself in the back-yard, and
comes back by a self-acting process in the morn-
mg; and as all these gentlemen have clamoured
to ns in the intervals of business, about Bunker’s
Hill, we wish to make one remark, and to make
it loud. We desire to impress on the great
American nation that it was licked —horribly
licked—at Bunker’s Hill, and that it is pretty
nearly the only nation that ever whooped for a
century on the strength of a lost battle and a
great, vigorous, unanimous bolt for a place of
safety. That is all for the present, except that
we have no doubt the new cyclorama will be a
brilliant success, and a joy and gladness to all
concerned, and that the lecturer will get his facts
so much mixed that nobody will know who won at
Bunker’s Hill, after all.

“Moths” is now attracting the attention of the
devout. Wybert Reeve is now sole lessee and
manager of this pretty little theatre, and has filled
all his dates for a considerable time with the best
productions obtainable. With Mr. Reeve’s long
experience and knowledge of the tastes of the
Adelaide public, he ought to make things hum.

Neil Burgess’ County Fair, managed by J. and C.
MacMahon, will soon introduce to Australia the galloping
racehorse effect (apparatus cost £500) in a racecourse
scene. Meanwhile Paris L’lllustratton, just to hand,
gives us a picture of the thing at local Theatre of
Vanetes; the stage has a moving floor, and three real
racehorses, with jockeys up, gallop for several minutes,
while a moving panorama at the back depicts the race-
course. Tub Bulletin described it many months ago.
Poor Paul Aubert, who perished miserably in Kevv
Lunatic Asylum, the other day, was a professor of fencing,
but unfortunately not in the Bungaree sense. He was a
master of the foil, the graces of the French school of
I’esornne. M. Meunier, Professor Miller’s father, used to
he the Melbourne dab in this line. Aubert acted with
Millie. Jeanne Andrbe and M. Munie, in their attempt to
introduce little French plays at the Melbourne Hall of
Science, then called something else, but they failed, like
the Sisters Navaro, and like Tessero’s ambitious troupe.
Good old Signor Zelman runs a high-class school of
music in Melbourne, and, with a degree of caution which
might well have been observed by a certain eminent
Italian flautist of old time, notifies in his prospectus that
“parents and guardians will be at libertv to accompany
the students both at the lessons and the classes.”
Dear Bulletin,- In your issue of 11th inst. you printed
a paragraph relating to the career of my late grandfather
(Richard Capper). In it you state that‘the residue of my
grandfather’s works would appear “in a future century.”
I should like to inform you that it is my intention at a
no distant date to publish a second volume of his dramatic
works (of which I possess the rights). Included in this
volume will be a descriptive drama founded upon events
that occurred in Hie earliest portion of Melbourne’s his-
tory, i.e., from 1835 to 1837. All the incidents in connec-
tion with the drama are founded upon facts, to one of
which my late grandfather was an eye-witness at the
time of the naming of the streets of Melbourne by Sir
Richard Bourke, then Governor of Port Phillip in 1835,
from which time, as you are aware, dates the founding of
Melbourne.—Yours, Alfd. Capper. Amess-street, N.
Carlton, 19th April, 1891.

“Hadn’t” Chambers writes to someone in Sydney con-
cerning his brother, Haddon’s play—“A dozen Royal
Nobodies have already seen it and condescended to think
it good, as well as numberless artistic lights whose
opinion was worth having, whilst the list of duchesses
with whom we swilled champagne on the stage after the
first-night performance would make the democratic hair

scarcely have imagined that either of the gifted pair was
massive enough to attract a British duchess.
