or Fred Neebe ?
He was one of those nightingale-like tenors whose
warbling of love-songs never fails to melt the female
heart. But when he was put outside a big hotel at a late
hour one night, owner only of half-a-crown, he was too
speechless to give his address. So the cabby drove him to a
suburban lock-up and they took him in. When he woke up
next morning his head was reposing on the clay-stained
bluchers of a sewer-sinker, and the bald head of an
ancient reprobate rested on his diaphragm. Soon after,
he was ushered forth to take a constitutional in “ Black
Maria.” Half the people in Sydney seemed to watch him
in—but, no matter! The bobbies thought they were
doing the swell a good turn when they put him away
from tiie roughs among the ladies. So did he at first.
Then the darlings opened out, and the “ old josser,” the
Domain “haybag” of forty years’ soaking, and the lav-
rikinness, just into her “teens,” strove who should
make him wince most deeply. “ Five bob or the rising,”
said the judge, and “ Correze,” who had only twenty
minutes to wait, thought it money well spent when he got
away at once through the ante of a kind friend.
J. H. Clyndes writes thus to a Sydney pressman:—“ I
am pleased to tell you that I have done splendidly since
my return from Australia. I had a tremendous three
weeks at the Standard 'theatre, with ‘Proof’and ‘lt’s
Never Too Late to Mend.’ My re-appearance in my old
part as Tom Robinson proved quite an event here) and
hundreds were turned away from the vast theatre nightly
(see cuttings). In fact, I should be perfectly happy here,
only I fear yodr climate has spoiled me. It has been ter-
ribly cold and foggy ! ! Oh, dear !”
It is probable that the Melbourne alligator would have
endeavoured to live a little longer had he known that a
baby-show was to be held in his vicinitv. The police are
so down on baby-farming, and it is otherwise so difficult
to lose babies.
The players in London music-hall orchestras complain
that they are expected not only to be musicians but
vocalists. They are expected now to aid certain singers
by bawling, “ Yes, you shall! ” “ No, you shan’t! ” and
other inane remarks to wake up the audience to participa-
tion in “ question ” songs.
Touring France once, Liszt had an audience of seven
only. Nothing daunted, he bowed suavely and said :
“ Ladies and gentlemen, this is a most uncomfortable
hall; therefore, if it will be agreeable to you I will have
the piano taken to the hotel where I am staying, and
there, where we shall be much more at our ease, i will
play through the programme.” The proposal was
accepted by the seven guests, who adjourned to the
hotel, where Liszt not only went through the entire
programme, but afterwards pressed his audience to par-
take of supper. The next evening, on the occasion of a
second concert, the hall was full to suffocation.
We regret to learn that Myra Kemble has again bean
seriously indisposed.
Irving intends shortly to revive “Charles 1.,” a play
which represents the “Martyr King” as a wingless arch-
angel and Oliver Cromwell as a blustering O.M.G. sort ol
person, who offers to burst up the Commonwealth if the
si rapine Charles will make him a marquis or an earl.
Eighteen years ago the gallery and pit had a vague sus-
picion that "Charles I.” was a put-up Tory job, and the
chances are that it will violently repudiate VV. G. Wills’
poetical lie when it comes to be told again. By the way,
Miss Lucia Harwood was one of the royal infants in
the original produclion.
A dismissed choir-leader revenges himself by sitting
close up to the tuneful sol-fa lot and singing so persistently
flat as to put the choir and whole congregation quite out
°f key. The parson threatened to take proceedings
against him. but found he had no legal remedy, and now
all they can do is to turn on the organ.
Gus. Harris manages the three biggest London theatres
—Her Majesty’s, Drury Lane, Covent Garden. Now, it’s
simple Euclid to say he is certain to go smash, on the
example of Chatterton, Ben Webster, and every other.
See Coleman’s “ Players and Playwrights” for how Drury
Lane, the Princess’, and Adelphi pulled Chatterton down.
About Irving in “ The Lyons Mail” it is remarked that
the original production in Paris had a happy ending, and
an unhappy ending, played on alternate mghts. The
only way to satisfy the clamour between the logical and
the sentimental. Bouoioault wrote “The Octoroon”
with the unhappy ending, but the London public made
him spoil it, and the case has been the same with Jones’s
“ Dancing Girl”—three splendid acts and a nonsensical,
sophistical fourth.
Frank Emery has a yarn of what happened once when
G. V. Brooke was playing “Richard III.” at Bolton. In
the Tower scene Gloucester says: “Go, Lovel, with all
sp°ed to Dr. Shaw! ” The rest of the sentence was
drowned in roars of laughter from a crowded house. Dr.
Shaw was the specialist of the locality*.

Windsor the Queen sent a telegram to D’Oyly Carte
hoping “ the company all got home safely.” How kind
and gracious and motherly—and childish !*
R. S. Smythe, the Much-Travelled, lives again in his
son, Carlyle Smith, B.A. One of the most marvellous
sights under Heaven is to note how the young dog has
picked up all the old dog’s tricks.
John Rogers, having discarded, or been discarded by,
Minnie Palmer, has taken to himself, dramatically, one
Annie Lewis, and is once more going on the road with the
old play, which he will now re-label “ My New Sweet-
heart.” A cast-iron cheek and a hard heart will do
wonders.
Barnum’s heirs have lost a big show by not exhibiting
him ere burial. Such lack of business capacity is enough
to make the old man come hack and disinherit them.
A nauseous little paragraph concerning the Duke of
Edinburgh and Sullivan’s “ Ivanhoe ” has been going the
rounds “at home.” It seems that a strange visitor called
at the stage-door of the new English Opera House while a
rehearsal of “ Ivanhoe ” was in hand, and asked to see the
composer. A workman standing in the doorway posi-
tively refused to rake the strange visitor’s pasteboard, on
the ground that Sullivan was not to be disturbed. Where-
upon the great unknown smiled in a pitying yet gracious
kind of way, and said; “ But, my good man, I’m the
Duke of Edinburgh.” The paragraph adds the startling
information, “And so he was," and then leaves the loyal
reader to turn pale as he thinks of the unfortunate work-
man flopping down upon the cold ground to crave for-
giveness of Victoria’s son. Tiie author of that choice
bit of literature shrank from attempting any sacrilegious
comment on the situation. He just concluded with
"And so he was,” which simple statement was evidently
considered to be a stroke of paralysis in itself.

Yosper, of the Republican (Charters
Tower-), is to be tried for “ sedition ” by a special
jury — i.e., a jury of monied men. His paper asks,
in its latest issue, how it happens that horses are
of more value in the world than men—that the
Queensland Government pays the squatters 7s.
daily for the hire of their horses, whilst the
hirelings -who ride them get only 6s. per day ?
In Chilian waters, the torpedoes have
beaten the ironclads with great slaughter. The
big ironclad will, as we always felt, disappear
from the face of the water ; meanwhile her sailors
will be, in continual tremor, like the inhabitants
of earthquake regions. America, with her
lightning cruisers and their dynamite guns, will
next year have the most powerful navy afloat; the
other fleets will be—are now, in fact—mere old
iron.
Editor Bulletin. —Sir, —ln re a paragraph in your
issue of 4th inst. regarding the death of Mr. J. M.
Macrossan, allow me to say that Mrs. Macrossan never
dispensed medical comforts (I understand this to mean
that she served in the bar of a public-house) to the Ravens-
wood diggers nor anywhere to anyone else, neither has
she ever been in nor seen Ravenswood in her life. The
fact of Mr. Macrossan having- received the sobriquet of
“ The Hatter,” which is given to diggers of a retiring dis-
position, will, I am sure, be sufficient to convince any
reasonable person that Mr. Macrossan was not in the
habit of frequenting public-houses; on the contrary (I
am now referring to both the Ravenswood and Charters
Towers diggings of which I can speak positively) ho
studiously avoided them,—Yours, &c., Ex-Ravenswood
Digger. Brisbane, 9th April, 1891.
At Callan and Son’s(George-sfcreet, Sydney)
annual exhibition of pictures, there is a very good
representative display by the N.S.W. artists.
Mr. Piguenit has met with the most decided
recognition in finding ready purchasers for some
of his pretty and artistic landscapes, the most
noticeable of which is “ A Spring Morning, Lane
Cove.” Mahony is well represented by a couple of
very clever colour-sketches, horsey f nd Australian.
Mr. J. R. Ashton shows, in our opinion, the very
best work that has yet left his easel ; “Mary
Magdalen,” a very fleshy life-study, and a charm-
ing, bright little picture, “A Ray of Sunshine,’
both in pastel, a medium which to h'm is evidently
most congenial. Mr. Lister-Lister is represented by
some clever and characteristic landscapes. Mdme.
Roth and Messrs. Hunt, Collingridgc, Minns,
Bevan, Fischer, Hanson and others show good
but not striking work. As years go on we do not
see that many of our Australian artists,, either
imported or of indigenous growth, feel the flavour
of the Australian soil. There appears to be a
lack of direct study from Australian nature, an
inability to obliterate from the mind the influence
in colour and technique of the masters of foreign
schools of pa nting, and to create a local and
original school' by. honest study of the life,
ch aracter andMenery of the land in,which we live-’.
Two pictures there are which will ever linger in
our memory ; One is Louis Buvelot’s “ Waterpool
at Colerainethe other Johnstone’S “ Off the
Track.” Each is the counterfeit presentment of a
piece of Australian life,

“ Bruce-street, Newcastle, March 28, ‘9l.
Gentlemen, —I wish to recommend your medicine
as a blood purifier. For some time my blood has
been in a very bad condition, continually breaking
out in boils, and when I bruised the skin, I had
great difficulty in getting it healed. My liver
was also very sluggish, and I was very much
troubled with headaches. I have taken onedar
of No. 1, and nearly finished a No. 2. Beyond
freeing me from headaches and increasing my
appetite, I felt very little benefit for the first
five weeks, and then I rapidly improved. The
boils have left off corning, my hands now quicklv
heal when bruised. I have had only one slight
headache since I commenced its use, and I feel
generally toned up all over. It appears to have
eradicated all impurities from my blood, for my
skin is now clear and of a healthy colour. I also
noticed, although the boils would not heal until
the impurities were driven from my blood, they
lost their soreness within a few days from the
time I commenced taking and applying your
Microbe Killer.—l remain, yours truly, Charles
Gardner. To Radam’s Microbe Killer Co.”

The Pestoralist's Last Will.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Price Preudergast owned freehold laud of many
miles extent,
And leased a spacious tract of downs at nominal
Crown rent;
His fame was great for wealth m sheep and cattle,
and he stood
Quite at the top among the swells of Pastoral
Brotherhood.
He never from the time he first drew breatli had
tasted wine,
Or any liquor brewed to make the head and heart
combine
To put a man outside himself, his selfish self, and
fill
Him as a fool, if nowise else, with human, large
good will.
Price lived beyond the windy hills, among the
rolling downs,
And ruled his kingdom with loud oaths and
beetled-visaged frowns !
He knew he held the purse of all, and whoso bade
him Nay
He ordered forth to starve and die upon the public
way.
His men from time to time found fault with some-
thing they thought wrung,
Forgetting that for every job a crowd kept st raight
along,
And knowing this Price called them fools and
gave them one day’s grace
To clear from off the hundred leagues he called
his private place.
His son, a soft, good-natured lad, called Tommy,
fell in love
With Julia Ann, a governess, at Murphy's, just
above;
And when Price heard the story he, without a
moment's rest,
Commanded Tom to quench the flame that burned
within his breast.
This, Tommy, like the average youth, refused
point blank to do,
Aiid as in night fires grow more bright, his love
grew stronger, too.
Till, though his father’s brow grew black, ho
married Julia Ann,
And, with curses fur his bridal gift, cleared out
a banished man.
Next, Price’s daughter, Jenny, breathed her love
unto the ear
Of Rex, the youthful storeman, whose duties
brought him near,
And for this dire offence was cursed and ordered
in disgrace
To hide her head for ever from her father’s angry
fu-
ll Happened, Tom and Jenny both encountered
hapless fates;
Tom lost an arm in crossing o’er between a rail-
way gates,
A iiu Rex fell sick of fever, and in the fever’s train
Was found to have contracted a disorder of the
brain.
Then Price’s wife cried, bitterly, “Oh, husband,
be nut stone!
Our children have offended you. but still they are
our own;
Oh, be not deaf unto their cries, extend a hand
and save—
For illness is a heavy cross, but Poverty, the
grave!”
Then Price rose stonnily and cried and cursed
and smote his wife,
And told her Earth and Heaven might change,
but not his mode of life ;
Whoe’er offended, crossed him once, should feel
his hate, till death
Removed the object from bis path and drank its
latest breatli.
Thus years went by and Price fell sick, and heard
the doctors say :
“ The time is very near at hand when you must
pass away;
Arrange your worldly things, and make your mind
up to depart
To where no break-up of tiie constitution stops
the heart. ”
The lawyer came and Price proclaimed to ali to
come and hear
Him make his will, so that his words might ring
in every ear;
And Jenny came, white-faced and weak, and one-
armed Tommy came,
The mother held a hand of each and all bowed
down in shame.
And Price raised up his head, and loud and firm
his voice was heard,
Dictating to the lawyer his last wishes, word for
word :
“ I never had a man by me,” he cried, “ who dealt
me straight;
No man shall, therefore, ever gain a cent, from my
estate.
“ No woman ever was true-blue, and, least of all,
my own;
My daughter and my wife have turned my heart
into a stone ;
The thing they call tiieir feelings set them up
against my will:
Hence, while they live they now must try their
empty mouths to fill.
“ All that I own I give to be sent round from
place to place,
The world around, for ‘gallowses’ to hang my
villain race.”
The will was drawn and duly signed, and Price
lay back and smiled,
And no one dared to speak to him—not even wife
or child.
He lingered thus throughout the night and studied
words to say,
So that a charge of madness no one might against
him lay,
And priests and parsons flocked around, each
trying to arouse
Him up to be a humane man to his own faithful
spouse.
But Price lay back and grimly smiled at all they
fain would say,
Repeating “Gallows,” “Gallowses,” until the
break of day.
Just then a horseman rode along, bound outward
to the west
And said he would feel grateful if allowed a little
rest.
Before he knew the trouble which afflicted the
abode
He gave one of the pasteboard-cards be used upon
the road ;
Tiie words on it were printed in script characters,
and ran
In one straight line, “The Venerable Father
Lan-i-gan.”
Despite the sorrow in the house he was made quite
“to hum,”
And soon was told the grief that was and what
was still to come ;
And having listened carefully to all they had to
say,
He begged that they would take him to the patient
straight away.
They brought him there, and when Price tried tiro
shaven face to scan,
A mouth said, “I’m the Venerable Father
Lau i-gan.”
WEAK WOMAN.
HUSBAND (just from turn): “Well, dear, you’re feeling better? Quite a colour.
WIFE (who has hem in the hands of every doctor in town): “Yes, I had a visit from the new
doctor.”
This made Price smile again and talk of “Cal-
lows,” “Gallowses,”
And Father Lanigan went back and asked for bis
valise.
“It’s Human Nature dead ails him,” lie whis-
pered, as he drew
A phial from the clothing which the rummage
brought to view.
He went back to the sick room, filled a glass half-
full, and said :
“Drink ! If human nature’s in you, this will stir
your heart and head!”
He drank. A while he shivered ; then he opened
wide his eyes;
He gazed around the bedroom with a look of
dazed surprise;
His wife ho called unto him, Ins daughter and his
son;
He kissed them and caressed them and he hugged
them one by one.
Then he wept just like a baby, and demanded
back the deed,
And tore it into fragments, and a new will straight
decreed ;
“I was a brute,” he whimpered. “Why, my
own must never need,
Nor swagman ever known to me feel hungry for a
feed !”
A new will was drawn emt, and while ’twas being
signed they thought
The venerable Lanigan a miracle had wrought;
But that great human student, after dinner,
drank the rest
Of his phial in hot water, with lump sugar, and
true zest.
The Whirligig: of Time.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
I crossed the old ford at the end of last May,
The old pub. had vanished, not even a shingle
Survived of the roof, which, in years passed
away,
Saw friendship and devilry strangely com-
mingle.
The few blackened wall-posts and panels of
fencing
That stand by the roadside, are all that
remain
To tell of old days ; a new era commencing
Has ended a gold-time we’ll ne’er see again.
Then Kate was the barmaid, as handsome a girl
As any line lady ; the smile that she gave
Had put heaps of poor fellows’ heads in a whirl,
And some poor unfortunates’ bones in the
grave.
She was worshipped, you see, by the coves all
about,
Who would fight like the devil her love to
secure ;
And Cupid, in those days, to settle a doubt,
Had a way of his own which was certain and
sure.
And Kate ! why she’d drive one insane with
her sighs,
With her pearly-white teeth and her lips red
as coral
She looked like a witch, in the depths of whose
eyes
The light sparkled best at the sight of a
quarrel.
I’ll always remember that Michaelmas morning,
When, down fro;;* the Bogong, young Archie
Mack ay
Stood joking with Kate, when, without the
least warning,
Flash Jim interfered in his coarse-speaking
way.
From Archie’s warm heart to the roots of his
hair
The fiery blood rushed, with a leap and a
hound ;
Like a flash he stood off, then a blow planted
square,
Sent Jim with a thud and an oath to the
ground.
With some terrible threats the two men closed
together,
A clinch and a struggle—Flash Jim did the
rest,
For, snatching his knife from its sheathing of
leather,
With Archie’s throat gripped, drove it into
his breast.
Flash Jim was the “ringer ’ of Moorabin shed,
And a bit of a bully—they hated him, all—
The crowd tried to rush him—he cowed them
instead
And stood panting at bay, with his back to-
the wall;
They wavered an instant, “Stand back, or by
God,” he
Exclaimed, “ who comes near me comes
straight to his death.
I’ll bury the blade of this knife in his body ;
So damn you, stand off!—let a cove get his
breath !”
Then he sped through the doorway and made
for the creek,
And the crowd with a shout followed closely
behind;
Lithe-limbed and lean-flanked, Jim could stay
for a week,
A real even-timer, he sped like the wind.
They chased him through timber, to where the
tall pines
Rose out of the sandhills, set close as a furze;
Right on to the range, where the setting sun
shines -
In a glory of crimson o’er ridges and spurs.
They lost him sometimes, till a stir of the
branches
Showed where he was threading the bracken
and fern,
And they followed like sleuth-hounds the trail
on his haunches—■
Each man as a bushman had nothing to
learn —
So hard on his track pressed the resolute band,
Impelled by a mixture of justice and passion;
And the flying man knew that lynch-law out of
hand,
Would follow his capture in summary fashion.
Archie’s mate, who throughout with a dogged
persistence
Had followed, stopped short, and without
much delay,
As he saw the tall ferns gently moved at a
distance
Not greater than ten or twelve paces away-
With tno skill that a sharpshooter picks off a
foe,
His revolver discharged—ere the smoke-cloud
departed,
A body rolled down through the brushwood
below,
Some twenty odd yards from the spot where
it started.
The flash, the report, its wild echoes resounding,
Fast summoned the crowd who, with ringing
“ halloo,”
Scrambled down to the creek, where, the victim
surrounding,
They found they had captured—a scrub-kan-
garoo.
Met bin ks I can call up the asinine change
Of expression which harassed their features
that day,
And hear in the silence that fell o’er the range
Jim’s wild peal of laughter die slowly away.
“ Where’s Archie ?” you ask. “ Well I guess I
am he.
’Neath the folds of my shirt, here, I still bear
the scar.”
“And Kate?” “Married years ago —married
to me—
And as handsome as when she served drinks
in the bar.
Here’s the landlord for orders—l’m dry with
this yabber;
Yours the same ; so is mine ; bring your own;
how he winks !
You were asking just now what became of the
stabber,
Flash Jim? Why, that’s he just gone out
for the drinks.”
T. H. ORD.
Stawell, Victoria.
The Case of Captain Dunbar.
Captain Frederick Dunbar, late of the Imperial
army, has now reached his eighty-seventh year
and has lived a life of great vicissitudes. In 1839
Captain Dunbar was invalided home after a long
residence in the tropics, the Medical Board de-
claring that his life would be forfeited with
another month’s residence in that malarial climate;
so far gone was his strength that the medical n.eu
advised the sale of his captain’s commission for
the benefit of his sons—which, as Captain Dunbar
puts it, he very foolishly agreed to, thus losing the
chancesof promotion which took place so frequently
during the Crimean war. Captain Dunbar has
lived in Australasia for many years, and has seen
some of the most stirring times known in these
colonies. He has witnessed the birth and growth
of the marvellous city of Melbourne, and would
have been one of Australia s wealthiest residents,
but for the unfortunate miscarriage of remittances
from Europe at the time of the first Government
land sale in Melbourne, the money being sent out
for the express purpose of purchasing land ; which
is at the present moment probably worth two
thousand pounds per foot!! Some years back
Captain Dunbar contracted a hernia caused by
over exertion in manual employment; a week
afterwards hydrocele set in. He underwent several
operations, all of which ended in no permanent
benefit, and the complaint finally ended in chronic
diabetes with its usual symptoms : excessive
secretion of water, aching limbs, headaches, loss of
appetite, weakness, &c. He read of the wonderful
effects of using Clements Tonic and commenced
its use, and with most satisfactory results; the
secretion of water decreasing almost simultaneous-
ly with the use of this medicine, the appetite re-
turned, and sogratifel was the captain that he
wrote to the pioprietors of Clements Tonic as
follows: “Rosewood, Ipswich, (Q.), Sept. 7th,
1890. Dear Sir, —Having taken several botdes
of Clements Tonic, I am happy to inform you
that it has resulted in a great benefit to my health.
I can now get a good night’s rest and great relief
from the diabetes which formerly troubled me so
much that 1 had to rise from bed every ten
minutes the night through, the amount of water
varying from eight to ten pints per night. It
now reduced to one pint, my appetite is good, and
I have no doubt that, with God’s blessing, a tew
more bottles of Clements Tonic will free me from
this great plague and my thanks will be due to
you. This letter being the pure truth, you, are at
liberty to publish it if you think fit. —Believe me,
yours faithfully, Frederick Dunbar. Captain
Dunbar will answer any enquiries if a stamped
envelope is sent with letter of enquity, but tne
most that enquirers can expect is that v»P«
Dunbar will say that the above account is true,
to expect more would be unr- asonable.

POLITICAL POINTS
The public debt of Australia is in all
£182,000,000.
You can only abolish luxury and poverty
by democratising property— Db Laveleye.
Federation is not dead ! No ;it has not
yet been born. It has only been conceived.
Out of 34 labor candidates put forward at
the late N.Z. General election 26 were returned.
Victorian Government now contains four
kwyers— Shiels, J. M. Davies, Duffy and Turner.
At least three members of the N.S.W.
Assembly are saving up their little £25 a month
for use at next election.
Alfred Deakin is said to have declined to
join the Munro Government against the instruc-
tions of Boss Syme, of the Age.
Alfred Deakin now says that any scheme
of federation must come endorsed by the deep-
toned “ aye ” of the millions of this continent.
One Atkinson remarks ni Twentieth
Century that a tax on land-values is a tax on
the expenditures that land attracts, not on the
produce.
The old fight of Charles Jardine Don and
I leales comes on again in the Victorian Legisla-
tive Assembly, with Hancock and Trenwith as
the pioneers.
Rats ! John Haynes has announced that
he can no longer support Parkes. Parkes on his
part repeats : “ Hi do not want heither the support
hor the haiquaintance hof Mister ’Aynes !”
Jenkins, S.A. Minister of Education, told
the Adelaide clergy last week that the Playford
Government would never agree to any system of
bible-reading being introduced into State schools.
Frank Stuart was not offered the Vic-
torian Commissionership of Customs because he is
a red rag to the Flinders-Lane bull. Premier
Munro is evidently a Scotch pedlar first, and an
alleged Liberal only second.
Says the Twentieth Century :
Under the proposed Single-tax all laud of any taxabD
value would presumably be in use. All land of no taxabl
value—vacant land-would be held out of use under paper
titles. How could the moneyless, landless man employ
himself any better under that system than now !
About 1000 Irish Tory females have signed
an address “to express condemnation of the
character of Mr. Parnell.” This document is
directed to the “Christian Women of Great
Britain,” which looks like a serious job for the
postman who has to find these parties.
Rees R. Jones, M.L.A., was Crown
Prosecutor against accused unionist shearers at
Barcaldine. Is not the position of Crown Prose-
cutor “an office of profit under the Crown? ’
If so, whom do we hear yelling for Jones’ resigna-
tion of Queensland Parliamentary membership ?
The Union Jack flies over the main camp
of shearer-harassing soldiery at Barcaldine, Q.
The Australian Flag waves over the unionist
shearers. Here we have the picture of the future
presented—a Foreign Domination v. Australian
Independence. May it be as bloodless as the
Shearers’ Strike!
John Redmond, M.P., in a recent letter,
alludes to J. G. O’Connor as the only Irishman of
any position whatever who when I visited Aus-
tralia in 1883 had the courage to come forward
and take a prominent part at the first meeting
which I held in Sydney. Now, didn’t Dan
O’Connor come forward ?
Barrister Maxwell, who just saved his
candidate’s deposit at Colling wood (Vic.) elec-
tion, is a member of the Scripture League,
Melbourne. Pious enthusiasts are said to be crow-
ing over the fact that Maxwell did save his ante;
yet he polled some 80 votes less than did Jos.
Symes, the atheist, his predecessor at the bottom
of the Collingwood poll.
A Sydney Sunday paper states that Bruce
Smith has written to Premier Parkes stating that
he cannot support his Federation scheme; and
that Parkes is throwing out his grappling-irons
for a coalition Ministry on a basis of ten per cent,
ad valorem. The Bulletin prophesied a year
ago that Parkes would ultimately attempt coalition
and still adheres to the idea.
fn all likelihood Parkes originally obtained
his “ One People One Destiny ” idea from Daniel
Webster. The great American, in a speech deli-
vered in 1837, made use of this phrase : “ One con-
stitution, one country, one destiny.” This, of
course, could not suggest “One people, one
destiny.” Oh, no. Will Lady Jersey kindly re-
head her poem, “ with apologies to Daniel
Webster”?
Edmund Barton said a nasty thing in his
speech at Manly the other day: that Dibbs’
“bomb-shell ” resolution to the effect that Sydney
should be declared the federal capital was sub-
mitted to the Convention in the handwriting of
Mr. Tom Slattery, who is a Robertsonian anti-
Federationist. Mr. Slattery explains that his
share in the matter was the putting of the Dibbs’
idea into parliamentary language.
The Peak Hill (N.S.W.) people have
pledged themselves in public meeting not to
support any Federal Constitution that had not (1)
the sanction of the whole of the people of this con-
tinent ; (2) such to be obtained on the one man
°ne vote principle; (3) to contain provision foy
the election of our own Governor; and (4) to pro-
tect the rights and individuality of each State.
Public opinion is always quite healthy among
gold-miners.
Says the Saturday Reviler (March 7): —
The object of all American commercial legislation is to
do all the harm it can to foreigners, and all the good in
its power, according to its lights, to the native citizen.
In this respect it does not differ from the legislation of
most other nations—or from what was our own down to a
comparatively recent period. We have given it up
because we found it did not pay us.
It is thus that the high-priests of the Cobdenish
fetish are dynamiting their shrine. Not a syllable
about the “ moral principle.” Profit’s the game !
As the cost of maintaining each unionist
shearer in the Barcaldine (Q.) strike camp is
under six shillings per week, it will take a long
siege to starve out the unions. It cost the
Pastoralists’ Association £5OOO to send 1000 black-
legs (Melbourne Argus figures) to Queensland,
and it has cost them at least another thousand to
transnort them to various stations. It is costing
the Queensland Government £lOOO a day to keep
the blacklegs at work, and to prevent them falling
on and exterminating the peaceful unionists The
last aspect is our own idea, and is quite as
reasonable as the biased pictures of the other side.
Parkes in October, 1884, when it suited
him to oppose Federation :
My hon. friend, Sir John Robertson, who has had so
much to do in the Government of this country, and who
has sat through all our Parliaments, is evidently opposed
to Federation altogether. Perhaps throughout the popu-
lation of this colony there is no man with a more power-
ful intellect or more capacity to grasp large questions
than the present Chief Justice, and Sir James Martin has
made no secret of the fact that he is altogether opposed
to Federation. He believes Federation would he a great
injury instead of good to these colonies. Here there are
two instances of men who have occupied very conspicuous
positions in the country, who by their large influences are
calculated to lead public opinion, and who are entirely
opposed to Federation.
Now is the time, if ever, for Alfred
Deakin to become the leader of the really demo-
cratic party of Victoria. He was wise to decline
Munro with thanks ; a re-coalition witli Gillies
should be impossible, even if Gillies be not a
hopelessly fallen Lucifer ; but Deakiu’s recent
difference with the late G. D. Langridge has never
been satisfactorily explained, and, as political
parties at present stand, this omission operates
with a section of so-called Liberals. The next
general elections in Victoria should not only re-
distribute parties, but should decisively divide
parties on a straight Labour issue. As for Sir
Bryan O’Loghlen, he always manages to cover
“FOR VALOUR.”
MAJOR : “For what engagements did you get all those medals and stars!”
VOLUNTEER: “Bicycle-matches, sir.”
himself and following with a cloud of ridicule
that makes him an unpalatability and at times a
negligeable quantity in politics.
By seizing a poor excuse to avoid laying
that foundation - stone of the Eight Hour’s
Memorial the Mayor of Melbourne gave evidence
of the contemptuous stand-offishness with which
Capital is treating Labour since the recent strike.
Although the old Pioneers seem to be playing a
lone-hand in the memorial matter, it was as
champions of the Trades that they were snubbed
by Mayor Lang. Time has its revenges in store,
however. Another year or two and the “repre-
sentative” persons will be tumbling over one
another in their eagerness to pay court to the
working-man. Even as it was, such careful
politicians as Premier Munro, Speaker Davies,
and President M‘Bain stood around and smiled
sweetly while old Ben Douglass tapped the stone
with his mallet. The failure of the Loan had
probably made that wily Trinity more than ever
anxious to propitiate all parties.
Old Ben Douglass, president of the
Pioneers’ Association of Melbourne, and layer of
the Eight-Hours’ memorial-stone last week,
whose denunciation of the “new ’’trades-unionism
has been received with such acclaim by the daily
papers, appears to be as mentally blind to the in-
evitable great future of Labour. Poor old Ben
is a sample of the ancient school of more or less
cap-in-hand workman, who was wont to beg as a
great favour what Labour now sternly claims as
a commonplace right, and what Labour will
enfoi’ce by latent power if it be not conceded as a
matter of justice. Ben shudderingly deprecates
the “ new ” unionism, and boasts that the “old ”
unionism never attempted to shook public opinion
by strikes, &e. Yet he is eloquent as to the
determination with which the “old” unionists
“ fought ” for their rights. Ben shrinkingly
bobbed up his head on occasion in political
contest, but he only “fought” after the fashion
of his school, and bobbed it down again—defeated.
His wandering prattle can do no harm to anybody,
albeit the daily press has seized it as a new text
against existent aggressive unionism. Ben can’t
stay the advancing tide, and the daily pre-s is
now only decently putting the coppers on his
sightless eyes.
Personal Items.
Yon Moltke, aged 91, is dead.
Barnum died worth over a million. Moral:
Always tell the truth.
A cable states that Henniker Heaton is
very ill in London of influenza.
Gladstone’s father started him in life with
a university education and a gift of £lOO,OOO.
During Captain O’Shea’s entire married
life (some 18 years) he spent 410 days at home,
says Parnell.
Minnie Breen, whose husband died at
Berrima, N.S.W., this week, aged 23, is only 13
years of age.
The Governor of Berrima (hi.B.W.) Gaob
ex-Sergeant John Paton, holds theV.C. for con
spicuous bravery.
“The Duchess of Montrose,” remarks the
Pink ’Un, “pays her chaplain £lOO per year and
her jockey £l5OO per year.
Salomons, Q.C., is a passenger per Mari-
posa from ’Frisco. He has visited Canada to
study the Dominion Constitution.
The defence of King George’s Sound is to
cost £15.561. Purves, Q.C., of Melbourne is not
on this job, though you might think he was.
Harry Chambers, brother of Haddon C.,
is also writing plays. We are sorry to hear this.
Shakespeare, for instance, had no brother in the
business.
Mr. Augustus Morris, of Manly, Sydney,
a year ago suggested the name “ Commonwealth ”
as the proper designation for an Australian
Federation.
Queen Victoria paid £l3O a day for her
hotel at Grasse. From which we gather that
somebody makes a little out of the British Crown
occasionally.
Annie Besan.t surprises the world by
giving a circumstantial account of Bradlaugh’s
former intimate association with the Fenian
organisation.
The Forrests are not the brainiest of the
Australian explorers, but tliey are the only lucky
ones. Look at Sturt, Stuart, Burke and Wills,
and all the rest.
It is cabled that two more members of the
British House of Commons have been arrested at
a Parisian bathing establishment charged with
unmentionable crimes.
Kirkton, one of the members for Ballarat,
says he would not object to having a lady colleague
in Parliament. You have guessed rightly. The
young man is still an ardent bachelor.
Parnell, now opposed by the Irish clergy,
openly declares that he would rather have seven-
years of Balfour’s administration, with all its
faults, than seven months of priestly tyranny.
Miss Ella Ewing, the handsome Amen-
caine, 18 years old, is the tallest girl living. She
weighs 19st. lOlbs., stands eight feet high, sleeps
in a ten-ioot bed, takes a 17 glove and a 19 shoe.
Sir George Grey must be an Irishman.
He said in Adelaide: “ I believe in the Single-
Tax—but not necessarily as being the only Tax.”
Result —a wild guffaw all over the wheat country.
Sir Samuel Davenport is a candidate for
the S.A. Legislative Council. He was a leading
S. A. politician 50 years ago when Sir George
Grey was Governor, and decided ,to re-embark in
politics during the veteran’s visit.
Sir Henry Parkes having absolved Dilke,
by letter, we are now waiting for H.P.’s letter to
Parnell assuring O.S.P. of H.P.’s firm belief in
his innocence as regards Kitty O’Shea. This
would heal the present Irish quarrel.
The Australasian gives an alleged portrait
of Coroner Youl, of Melbourne, a man whose
proud boast is that he has held over 11,000
inquests. The libellous picture makes him look
like a man who has committed over 11,000
murders.
It has been discovered by James Runci-
man {“ John A’Dreams ”) that Grace Darling was
not the fairy-like beauty she has been painted,
who talked like a lady and sang like a bird, but a
stiff-built, raw-boned, round-shouldered, stolid
girl whose teeth were bad, whose freckles were
numerous, and wh so language was at once free
and obscure y local. Another idol shattered.
The abolition of the Indian Opium traffic
is agitated for, but as the Indian Government
expoits 12 millions sterling worth of opium
annually to China and elsewhere—why, you
might a» well attack the British beer-trade.
Never will an Englishman do what the Chinese
commissioner did—destroy £2,000,000 worth of
opium, to prevent his country from being
corrupted.
Somebody was recently privileged to in-
spect Queen Victoria’s bed. “ The guide drew our
attention to the hair mattress and to any number
of little strings protruding from the envelope.
‘You see,’ said the man, ‘every morning these little
things are untied, and every evening they are tied
up again, in order that the padding may be more*
comfortable. It takes her maid two hours every
day to do this work.’”
F. M. Mansel, formerly on Governor
Kintore’s staff, who was found dead with his head
crushed on a railway-line at Melbourne the other
day, was intellectually too clever for an aide-de-
camp-ship and intended to go in for politics when
he returned to England. Mansel was son of an
English parson, and very popular amongst
Adelaide people. His death is shrouded in
mystery, and the facts point to suicide.
A London paper asks (obviously apropos
to the Wales-Hirsch notorious chumship):—
Is a certain Royal residence mortgaged to a certain
foreign millionaire for £400,000 or any sum ? (And if so,
does the fact account for some extraordinary intimacies
lately very publicly commented upon?) Will the “ assets”
of a certain city firm, more or less supposed to have
“ gone under,” include a debt of £‘250,000 from a certain
personage who shall be nameless, and which sum or any
part of it cannot be got at any price?
Ploji Plon Prince Napoleon’s expiry was
edifying, somewhat recalling Carlyle’s de ; cription
of Louis XV. going off the hooks, with a courtier
putting his sword to the priest’s throat to compel
him to administer extreme unction. Plon Plon
began his defiant atheism by a roaring supper
with Sainte Beuve and Renan, on a Good Friday,
over 30 years back, fearfully scandalising Eugenie,
and making Badinguet wince. At his death bed
a cardinal persuaded him to mumble something to
an abbe, and so he was buried in church, but
probably almost the last thing heard from him
was, “I never succeeded in anything, and can’t
succeed even in dying.” He declared that he
died an Emperor adhering to the principles of the
Concordat, and fully imbued with the religious
sentiments, of the Bonapartes.
The much-vaunted Conservative “victory”
in Canada was the same old Conservative swindle
over again. Canada is sick of slippery “Old To-
morrow” Macdonald; it is tired of the unprofit-
able “loyalty ’ which is ruining its trade by post-
poning the inevitable customs union wth the
United States, and it is weary of a good many
other tilings besides. But the “annexation party ”
consists of the young men of the country, and
Macdonald disfranchised over 100,000 of them at
one jump by using the electoral rolls which were
got up more than two years ago, instead of a new
list revised up to date. Consequently, every man
who has come of age within the last two years
was not allowed to vote, for he had no chance to
register, and a lot of the men who had died since
1888 did vote, for there was no chance to take
their names off the list. It was a clever swindle,
but still the Conservatives got back to office only
by a hair’s breadth.
Premier Griffith’s impenetrability to
humour was basely taken advantage of by Con-
ventionist Gordon from South Australia—the
same man who in a mock-deferential way so neatly
castigated Parkes. Several delegates were Grif-
fith’s" guests aboard the -Queensland Government
steamer, Lucinda, and the boat passed at some
distance the N.S.W. “war-ship,” the Wolverene.
Gordon seriously approached Griffith, and said,
with perfectimmobilityof countenance: “Couldn’t
you manage, Sir Samuel, to arrange for these two
ships to fire at each other for a little while - to
oblige your guests, who have deputed me to make
the request ? It would be h'ghly educative, and
ijrivo us all a realistic idea of a naval engagement.
The N.S.W. Government would probably bear all
the cost.” Griffith was simply dazed with shocked
amazement. “My dear fellow,” said he “I
couldn’t dream of such a thing. It would paralyse
all Australia. It’s quite impossible.” Gordon
wandered away gloomily—with his tongue in his
cheek, while the other Conventionists ran behind
the funnel to laugh.
Adelaide has a perennial insolvent in one
W. E. Adcock, who ran a bic business in Mel
bourne with a branch in the Northern Territory,
and bankrupted as to the latter. The Adelaide
Insolvency Court can t be good at figures, for it
cruelly professes to want to know about a lot of
items which it argues display a leakage of some
£14,000 or so. Adcock is willing to erupt crater-
fuls of figures, but he can’t satisfy the Court,
which sent him to rusticate in the gaol over two
years ago. In every statement he makes to the
Court, Adcock’s little joke is that he is “domi-
ciled in Victoria,” though he lives in Adelaide
gaol as stated. Just before Christmas last,
Adcock applied to be liberated on bail, in order
that he might spend the festive season with his
friends in Melbourne. The Court insisted upon
very heavy bail, hut he found it, and went over
to Marvellous Melbourne for a rattling good time.
He has since returned to Adelaide and surrendered
himself; enjoys himself in gaol writing bad poetry
and worse prose, and is getting fat on his confine-
ment, which is indefinitely “ during the pleasure
of the Court.” From appearances, Adcock can
stand it as long as the Court can.

The Australian Star, in stating, per
medium of three separate paragraphs in the same
column, that Bruce Smith, the N.S.W. Minister
of Works, has been “ excluded from his late
father’s will,” that he has been “disinherited,”
and “cut off with a brass farthing under the will
of his much-respected father,” merely displays its
ignorance of a fact well known in commercial
circles ; that when Mr. Bruce Smith withdrew
from the family concern of Howard Smith & Co.,
Limited, some years ago, he t ok with him his
fair share of the family wealth. That being so, he
could hardly expect to come in for another
“ hand.”
* * #
The public consider that, under all the
circumstances of the case, the N.S.W._ “Cove”
doesn’t gain much glory by communicating to the
press the particulars of interviews he has with
Lady Jersey, unless those interviews take place
at his own villa. However, this being a free
country, people are at perfect liberty to indulge
their vanity at the expense of their self-respect.
# * *
Billy Elton, comedian, as is pretty well
known, we believe, is a bit of a humourist. What,
perhaps, is not so widely known is that he has a
little son of about five years who attends a Vic-
torian State-school, and recently surprised the
prim female-tutor by pleading : “ Please, will you
sit me between two nice girls to make me
modest?” It is alleged that young Billy was put
up to promote this social scandal by William the
elder before he left breakfast for school,
# # *
When Bishop Moore, of Ballarat, called
into the condemned cell of Cornelius Bourke the
day before he was executed, -with the laudable
purpose of trying to make him fix his thoughts on
the next world, Bourke immediately gave an easy
turn to the conversation by asking the Bishop “ if
he had a match on him.” The Bishop evaded the
question. This convinces us that he is not a
genuine believer in the doctrine of eternal punish-
ment. If lie had been he might have told Bourke
to wait another day and he would— failing
repentance—be able to light his pipe with hi?
finger. A member of the Melbourne Presbytery,
or any other good old Calvinist, wouldn’t ha-e
missed such an obvious point. s
A well-known Melbourne architect started
connubial bliss under difficulties, last week. tin
the day preceding the joyful event he fell off a
roof, and a deputation waited upon his bride-elect
to learn whether a husband with a broken leg,
l icked back, and sundry contusions would do all
right as he was, or had he better be held over for
repairs. The lady was unanimously in favour of
the ceremony proceeding, because the fatted calf
had been slain and all her things were ready.
Alsu the death of one of her intended bridesmaids
only a fortnight previously had shown the m cessity
for prompt dealing in such matters. So the wed-
ding-bells rang out a merry peal, the bridegroom
was earned into the tabernacle by four staggering
friends, and the solemn service, interrupted only
by two fainting-fits on the part of the “best
man,” went along as usual. After the groom had
groaned “I will,” the four friends shifted his
b oken bits into the vestry that he might be duly
Mgned, sealed and delivered to the queen of his
heart, and then they carried him down the aisle
to the bridal chariot, just, for all the world, as
t hough they were bringing home her warrior dead.
No doubt the heroine ordered all things for the
best. No true Australian girl is happy until she
gets him, and if she gets part of him m plaster of
Paris, and the rest in poultices and eucalyptus oil
—why, it only proves that she wanted him badly.
# * *
In the course of pulling down the old
Svdney markets some very interesting relics of
ancient times were brought to light. These,
instead of being retained by the civic authorities
and kept among the Town Hall records or handed
over to the Australian Museum, seem to have
been distributed - among collectors. Who, for
example, got hold of the records contained in a
bottle buried with the foundation-stone 70 years
ago?
* * *
The most radiant-looking personage seen
in Sydney for five years was T ger Inglis, at the
Sydney Chrysanthemum Show, who escorted
Lady Jersey round to see the exhibits. There
were others, however, who were not so delighted.
One of these was Mr. C. M. G. who is so particular
about having his front and back names hitched
together with a hyphen. He looked askance
at the proceeding, and his private opinion would
have been well worth having.
* * *
Where were the new Mayor and Mayoress
of Sydney, on the occasion of the afore-
said Chrysanthemum Show? Two Earls or
a Countess in the hall, and no mayoral
dignitaries to bask in the light of their pre-
sence! Shades of Croesus Burdekin, how could
this happen ? Perhaps someone will ask a question
in the Council about it.
a * #
Anglican Bishop Camidge, of Bathurst, m
his pastoral address to his Diocesan Synod, the
other day, beseeches the laity thus : —“Above all,
never let your clergyman be troubled with pecu-
niary matters.” Would it fill the bill to feed
them, clothe them, lodge them, and give them no
at all? “Above all” is distinctly
suggestive in the mouth of a bishop.
* # #
The Brisbane insolvency-epidemic is, of
course, laid to the door of the strike, and not to
the sharks’ land-boom of three or four years ago.
Business in Brisbane has been for years conducted
on a rotten basis, and the strike is really a wel-
come scapegoat to long-tottering houses to whom
“ the Court ” is a haven of relief and peace.
* # *
For ballot in a Sydney club, a new
candidate for membership has been proposed.
“Is he in business, or what?” asks one of the
fathers of ihe club. “ He’s cashier in the Imperial
Bank,” repli* s his sponsor. “He’s made a mis-
take ; this is a club, not a gaol,” retorts the old
’an, cynically.
* # #
That well-known speculator, Edmund H.
Taylor, now incarcerated at Darlinghurst for
alleged false pretences involving a large amount,
was brought to Sydney Supreme Court last week
m the custody of—the gaol-flogger! to give
evidence in a civil case. Surely the unfortunate
Taylor, who is not a brute, might have been
spared that hideous indignity. Why didn’t the
authorities, while they were about it, turn over
the imprisoned man to the custody of the nose-
less executioner? Can it have been only because
“Nosey Bob ” doesn’t happen, like the flogger, to
be a gaol-warder ?

discovering that his old aunt, who recently died,
had left all her money to an aged sister, with the

* * *
One of that well-known ham-fat family, the
“Big Joyces,” of Toowoomba, Q., is at Mildura
(Vic.) irrigation-colony, the locals whereof
seem surprised that Bananaland can produce a
“26-stunner.” The specimen they have is one
of three brothers, all Daniel Lamberts, nearly
Gft. high and over 20-stone each. Other pecu-
liarities they possess are smo jth faces and squeaky,
treble voices, that come as surprises from such
prodigious masses of humanity. And “thereby
hangs a tale.” Once a “giant family” of fat
persons visited Warwick, Q., and proposed to go
on to Toowoomba. The three Joyces were in
Warwick and went to see the “giants.” Being
introduced to a “giant” youth, said to be 17 years
old, and weighing some 10 stone, the Joyces dis-
sembled. “Seventeen?” said Squeaker No. 1,
“why, I’m only 14; what has he got to blow
about?” “And I’m only 12h,” added Squeaker
No. 2. “ And I’m just turned 10; I’m the oldest
of us three,” remarked Squeaker No. 8. The
showman, who was trotting them around, became
agitated, and asked —“Really? Where do you
hail from?” “Natives of Toowoomba.” “Will
you accept an engagement?” “An engagement?
Not much. Why, we’ve a fine stud-breeding
place of our own up there. Besides, we’re only
chickens to half the Toowoomba p» ople. We’re
perfect pigmies to the big ’uns there. If you’ve
any notion of exhibiting this family in Too-
woomba, you’d better run ’em as dwarfs !” -The
showman that night folded up his moveable
caravan and silently stole away. He did' not
visit Toowoomba.
# # #
An Australian lady visiting England writes
thus :
We went to Portsmouth and saw the big warships
launched, getting tickets of admission to the dockyaidon
the strength of being “ colonials.” We had a splendid
view from start to finish. The Queen was not cheered at
all; she never glanced to right or left, looked as sour as
vinegar, and seemed in great haste to get the ceremony
over. Am sending a paper containing occount of pro-
ceedings.
The said printed report (Daily Chronicle) differs
somewhat from the written one, for it says :
The reception of the Sovereign was very cordial, and
she was evidently pleased with it. ..." The cheering
became louder and louder, and the Queen’s face brighter
and brighter. ... Her Majesty was conducted to her
carriage amid immense cheering.
The discrepancy is, to take a charitable view,
accounted for in the last paragraph, thus
All the arrangements of the day were excellent, with
the exception of those for the Press, than which nothing
could be more imbecile if they were not designed for
deliberate obstruction. This is not the first time of late
in Portsmouth Dockyard that representatives of the great
journals of the country have been invited only to find
that they are not expected to see and verify what takes
place.
Evidently the powers that be deem it better that
reports of such proceedings should be slightly in-
accurate, or, as in the present case, mendaciously
untruthful, than that the public should know what
actually occurs.
A Bermagui (N.S.W.) schoolmistress was
recently alarmed at a late hour by the knocking
and kicking of a wild-eyed lunatic at her front-
door. Binding expostulation vain, she, with her
sister, vacated her downy couch and departed by
the back-gate in search of male assistance.
Returning with a large-sized man, they were
staggered to find the invader of the pedagogic
peace peacefully snoring in their bed. He was
quickly ejected by the champion of lone woman-
hood, who shortly after went home, where, to his
surprise, he found the maniac from way-back
calmly sitting down to supper in his back kitchen.
* * *
About twenty-five years ago a little boy
and girl, aged about ten and eight years respec-
tively, told the Melbourne police an awful story.
They had, they said, just walked all the way from
Woodstock (about 30 miles). Their father had
come home drunk, murdered their mother with
the wood-axe and burnt the body. This yarn
they stuck to, and after it had been taken down,
troopers rode in hot haste to Woodstock (there
was in those days no telegraph)—only to find the
father and mother well, but much concerned at
the disappearance of their children. The father
informed the police that he had strapped his
progeny the day they ran away, but what had
put murder into their heads he could not under-
stand. Both these promising brats, it is interest-
ing to know, have since compiled pages of their
own in the criminal records.
* * *
At funerals such as Sir F. Murphy and
Chief Secretary Langridge, one sees empty
carriages sent by their owners as a tribute of
Respect 1 . Suppose anyone sent a dray, or a
butcher’s cart, what an insult? But a carriage,
properly varnished, escutcheoned, silver-mounted,-
is another thing in the sleight of hand which
makes up a funeral from which a sweet savor
ascends on high.

One of the droll Melbourne institutions is
the Boys Saturday Night Pigeon market. There
you will see 500 boys, each with a pigeon under
his coat, on the trade and swap.

» # #
A new sassiety idea has been struck by “a
well-known London hostess,” who recently gave a
dinner to a crowd of bachelor dudes, and induced
a number of high-born angels to dress themsCves
up as parlor-maids and carry the victuals round.
This fashionable foolery was such a brill ant
success that another dinner is arranged at which
the girls are to figure as guests, whilst the chappies
appear as pampered menials in plush. By these
means Adolphus de Vere will be able to take the
servant-girl on his knee without loss of dignity,
and the bon ton belle will hug the counterfeit
presentment of Jeames in a perfectly frank and
open manner. We regard the new idea as a great
boon to the nobility and gentry, whose daughters
may from this time forward derive all the social
advantages attaching to a “good match” with
the delirious joy permeating an elopement with
the flunkey.

otherwise.
* # #
A large number of ladies who visited
Campbelltown (N.S.W.) encampment declared
that Captain M‘Neill, of the 4th Hus-ars, but
at present attached to the N.S.W. cavalry at a fat
screw, was “ the handsomest man they had ever-
seen.” Some men won’t admit the gallant cap-
tain’s claim, but that, of course, is mere preju-
dice. Butting aside his claims to manly beauty,
he certainly is just the biggest “ to# ” in' the Aus-
tralian military service. His full-dress tunic is a
marvel, his undress ditto is trimmed with
astrachan, and his mess waistcoat is completely
covered with gold embroidery. Mentally he is
understood to be not quite equal to Shakespeare,
but, perhaps, it would hardly be wise to tell him
to to his face, for he looks six feet-two, three, or
four, in his socks, while on horseback. The captain
is said to be married ; the event having occurred
some time before he knew he as coming to
Haw-aw-stralia, and lias an amiable weakness for
singing hunting and bacchanalian ballads in a
very pipey voice and doncherknow style.
# it -»
An American girl with a good figure, who
has an extremely slender waist and laces it to its
possible limit, at a recent ball wore a white kid
bodice. Thekid had been damped and then sewn on,
so as to have no visible fastening. The result was
that after sitting in a warm room for two hours her
figure was as neatly encased as if in a tight-fitting
glove. The same damping process was followed
with regard to the tiny white shoes, which were
made to fit her bare feet tightly, and thus ensure
a very good fit over stockings. She did not un-
dress for the two days, resting on a sofa for the
night, and wearing a thin dress over her white kid
bodice in the day. She was “immensely ad-
mired. ”
* * *
In a recent English baby-farming case it
transpired that a woman who had never had any
children passed off a “ bought ” baby upon her
husband as his own. She lay up for the occasion
and fooled her husband so successfully that until
she confessed, the day before attending court as a
witness, he w’as not undeceived.
* * *
Mr. Poet Southey somewhere mentions
that in days gone by the Mayor of Leicester used
to be chosen by a pig. The candidates sat m a
semi-circle, each one with his hat full of beans in
his lap, and he was elected Mayor from whose
hat the pig ate first. A modification of this civic
custom, The Bulletin notices, widely prevails
4o this day. The Aldermen’s vote is almost
always cast, for the man who has most swill in hie
trough and is willing to give it away freely.
Two facts are much in evidence at the
N.S.W. April Assize Courts. First, that the
number of capital offences is without precedent
for many years past, and further that juries will
not find on the capital counts, but wriggle out
on the minor charge if they have to convict.
Murder is toned down to manslaughter, and rape
discounted to assault with intent. Juries won’t
find guilty on now capital charges once in ten
times that they ought, nor will they until capital
punishment is numbered with the hasbeens.
# * #
The captain of the ship Hollingwood,
which recently touched at the Pitcairn Islands,
says that the youngest men in the group enquired
from the sailors what was the size of a full-grown
horse. As they have never seen horses at Pitcairn,
there is no local need for gaols.
* * #
At Zeehan, Tas., recently a Progress
Association was elected by vote. Next day a
newly-fledged member was busily engaged in play-
ing “ two up ” in the main street, the centre of an
admiring crowd, while strict watch was being kept
for the man in blue.

“ And what da you think the plaintiff did
next, gentlemen ? He used an oath, gentlemen
an oath so vile, so villainous and revolting—an
oath so immoral and blasphemous, gentlemen,
that if I were to try to repeat it my lips would
rebel against its syllables and refuse to voice so
hideous and horrible an expression. What do
vou think that oath was, gentlemen? It was:
‘Well, I’ll bed d.’ Think of that, gentlemen!
Extract from the speech of the only orator at the
N.S.W. bar. And it reminds us that once when
the" late David Buchanan was addressing a
Southern jury their foreman interrupted the
oration by asking : “Your Honor, are we obliged
to listen to this rot ? ”

The Melbourne trams in 1889 carried
44,556,404 passengers, equal to about 50 trips per
head of its city population, or £556,955 in hard
cash.

Gov. Hopetoun is disillusionised. Where
now are the gorgeous flunkies and the absurd
carriages ?
* # *
Have you ever noticed that when a child
is born unto a clergyman the religious papers
invariably speak of the infant as “a little son
or “ a little daughter ” —as if to palliate the
offence? #
Something worth seeing was that ceremony
at Melbourne railway-station when Grey, of
Maoriland, and Munro, of Victoria, arrived from
the Convention. There was a shouting multitude
to welcome Grey, and the old pro-consul
looked like a man about whom a nation might
reasonably enthuse. But, as for Munro, he was
the ideal of a bagman who come back from an
unsuccessful trip, and as he jammed his hat down
over his commonplace head and bolted tor a cab
with his samples in his hand, he looked a very
miserable bagman, and one that travelled tor a
firm in an exceedingly small way of business.
,5* * *
muiioer of exuoorant persons, male
and female of their idiotic kind, in a certain Mel-
bourne suburb, decided to give a surprise party in
the house of a mutual friend the other evening.
They arrived to find that another surprise party
had got in ahead of them and was in possession of
the house. He was a bailiff. Some of them then
began to see that their visit might be construed
as an indelicate intrusion on the privacy of
domestic life, but two or three felt the imbecility
of their position so keenly that they clubbed
together and actually bought out the bailiff and
held their party in the house after all. Their
host was geniality personified and entertained
them royally on their own provisions and liquor
till dawn, with velvet foot, came steal ng o’er the
eastern hills and brought the milkman whopping
on his rounds. It was only discovered by accident
a few days afterwards that the whole thing was a
“plant.” The impecunious one had got wind of
their intentions and induced an acquaintance, not
known to any of the party, to personify a bailiff,
which he did with great success, as previously
stated.
We hear some curious statements occa-
sionally as to the methods adopted to obtain
apparently influential names upon the Boards of
management of public companies. Here is a fact
for which we can vouch. A prominent citizen,
holding a seat upon the Board of one of the prin-
cipal Australian Life offices, resigned, as he was
going to England. An emissary of the office

-luo uurmg and adventurous trip of
Governor Kiutore across the continent is, we
learn from the daily papers, progressing favour-
ably ; in fact, “ doing as well as can be expected. ”
Mr. Todd “ holds daily telegraphic conversations
with his Excellency.” Happy Mr. Todd ! Kintore
is also kept posted up in all latest scandals, home
and foreign, also, the name of the latest British
M.P. who has bolted to avoid a prosecution ; and
the state of health of Her Most Gracious. He
has a field-operator with him, so that the line can
be tapped at every convenient shackle that is
passed. What beneficial result is to accrue to the
Territory from a new-ehum with a raw Scotch
accent travelling along a road constantly traversed
by teams and line-repairers is hard to fathom. He
might as well travel on an exploring expedition
from Sydney to Parramatta. When the late
Duke of Manchester paid that portion of Aus-
tralia a visit, he had several little business specs
in his pocket. Has Kintore the same ? The
Northern Territory has long been the grave of
defunct syndicates and moribund companies; is
the noble Earl going to resuscitate some of these
and place them on their staggering legs once more?
So far, the only .object achieved has been the
banqueting of the Governor by the Chinamen of
Port Darwin, which, however gratifying to the
recipient, is scarcely fraught with much in-
terest to the rest of Australia. The vice-regal
sympathy with Mongolian disabilities will not
meet with much response in Australia proper. So
Kintore proceeds on his exploring expi dition along
a main road, and the Elder expedition starts to go
over country which is now almost equally well
known; and the sun of Australian exploration
sets in clouds of ridicule.

Lord Justice Bowen, in an essay on legal
history, remarked that, while the old Court of
Chancery was an almost unrivalled instrument
for the discovery of truth, it had, however, one
drawback; “The truth was seldom discovered
until everybody interested in it was buried.
There is a Melbourne citizen ess (she bears an his-
toric name), living in Balaclava, who will get
£150,000 when a suit which dates from Bingham’s
Chancellorship is settled. She is 73, and “theold
forms ” rule the procedure in the case, so, by the
time she’s 130, she expects to be told she may
expect her money in 20 years.
More “ schduffing.” “ Kallara ” writes
of a time when he was shearing at W. Station
and the Scotch cook getting drunk, Wa Tee was
engaged as the only possible substitute. “ Kallara ”
had swelled head bad first day of Wa Tee's reign
and lay in his bunk all the morning. What he
saw was Wa Tee making rolypoly pudding. All
went along slick, till Wa Tee suddenly stopped,
looked round, scratched his head, muttered
“Wotfoh? No savee. All li!” dashed to his
bunk, pulled out a big heathen shirt; rip went
the same, and before “Kallara” could utter a
swear-word the rolypoly pudding and about half
the Chinee-shirt were in the pot mixing their
mutual mellownesses according to chemical laws.

A CRUSHER FOR JOHN.
HUSBAND: “ I see the Government has decided to appoint a Royal Commission to enquire
into the relations between employers and employed.”
WIFE: “ I’m astonished, John, at your mentioning such a matter to me.”
HE (feebly) : “My dear, I don’t quite understand.”
WIFE (in sepulchral tones): “I was thinking about your relations with that last hussy of a
parlourmaid.”

Mem. for the consideration of the Com-
mercial Banking 00. of Sydney. At Kiukiang,
China, recently, some forged bank-notes were
refused payment by the manager, who was there-
upon promptly brought before a magistrate. T- his
Solomon ruled that, since the writing and marks
on the false notes were so like those on the regular
bank-notes that ordinary people could not dis-
tinguish between the good and the forged, the
bank must meet them ! Here one is supposed to
laugh. Reflect a little, though, and the decision
is not so cranky as it at first appears. Why
should not the law insist upon specially-privileged
chartered banks issuing notes which cannot be
so readily imitated as to deceive ordinary people?

The Little Sisters of the Poor—a Mel-
bourne Papal organisation —houses Protestants,
and has built a Northcote Retreat, costing
£30,000. Very good, in its way, but really we
don’t want this kind of thing, any more than Italy
and Spain. The Little Sisters are excellent, but
big, fat men get meals on the strength of them.

Annie Claxton, a Melbourne maiden with
a heart bowed down, has just obtained an order
for thirty shillings a week, with two guineas costs,
from the ex-object of her idolatry. He bears the
misleading name of Frank, and before they left
of? looking at the moon together, and wondering
what star would be their particular brick villa
when love becomes immortal—in those happy,
happy times he borrowed £2O from Annie as a
token of her affection. Frank cooled down in due
course, and Annie took proceedings with the
result aforementioned. Her sad, reproachful ex-
pression probably stood her in lieu of any I O U’s,
otherwise the young man would scarcely have
alleged that “plaintiff proffered him the money.”
But Frank was very properly “reprimanded by
the Bench for his manners,” and withdrew from
court in a crushed condition. —

OWED TO ANNIE.
Ah ! Annie, in the days of old
You gave to Frank the yellow gold,
Nor cared much how he spent it—
Enough that he was all your own,
And when he wished to raise a loan.
Without a groan you lent it.
He loved you so, or so he said ;
You never asked to be repaid,
That notion you derided :
For was ho not your other life
From whom, when you became his wife,
You ne’er could be divided ?
Your face upon his bosom hid,
You anted-up each little “quid ” ■
And never felt you missed it;
The little hand that held the brass
He fondled in his paw, alas !
And, Judas-like, he kissed it.
Methinks we see you from your gown
Produce a gleaming half-a-crown.
And beg him to absorb it,
The while that Venus up above-
Sweet sympathetic star of love—
Progressed along her orbit.
You put your trust in Franky then,
You little knew the ways of men—
How mean they are and grasping;
You gazed into his god-like eyes,
And, as he told the good old lies,
With happiness were gasping.
But O ! there came another day :
He parted, but he would not pay
The money you advanced him.
You found it was the chinking pelf
And not, and not your little self
That formerly entranced him.
His tender heart grewjnighty tough,
He kept on “ sticking to the stuff ”
(With which the Devil wins man),
He left you weeping in the lurch,
He would not take you to a church,
Nor yet to Mister Kinsman.
You brought him up before the beak,
And thirty “bob” each blessed week
Must Franky now be sporting.
When spooning in the days sublime
You counted not upon a time
When you’d go county -courting.
The Higher Life.
As a writer un social topics, in a style that is
elevating in thought, as well as elegant in diction,
Mrs. Lynn Lynton has achieved a decided repu-
tation. In her article on the religion of Self
Respect in the New Review she makes a bold
but justifiable attack on the money-grabbing
tendency of the times. She seeks to awake to
new consciousness the moral sense of the apparent
worthlessness of mere gold-worship. “ After all,”
she says, money is not the end of life, for when
death comes our money is of no avail. This
rather is the true end of life; to try to lead a life
of happiness, not of pleasure ; to do right because
it is right, to do right even though it may appear
to other people wrong, even though it may be
contrary to all accepted maxims of our com-
mercial world. Do right regardless of what
people say, and, if necessary, defy society. We,
of to-day, boast of our liberty, but we are the
most miserable slaves the world has ever seen.
In every little detail we are mercilessly ordered
by these two tyrants —Custom and Society.”
This is, if merciless, to a great extent too true,
and it is also true especially of our own land, but
there are large numbers, and the numbers are
constantly increasing, who think for themselves,
who will no longer be content to pay slavish obedi-
ence to the mandates of Custom and Society.
They will not believe what their intelligence
controverts, simply because “ everybody says so;”
they will not, do what does not seem right,
because “ everybody does so.” They swing out
from the beaten paths of creed and custom to find
“greener woods and pastures new” of thought
and action. So come to tne world new inventions
to ease the burden of labor, new remedies to cheat
the yawning grave of its victims, new and better
notions of existence in the life wh'cti is to come.
If there were no original thinkers the sun would
still circle through the heavens, but the world
would stand still. There are medical men who still
cling to the “ bleed and blister ” theory ; there are
some who pooh-pooh at the idea that Warner’s
safe cure is a specific for kidney disorders, solely
because it is a proprietary remedy—they are back
number editions. The most intelligent of the
profession, men like Dr. R. G. Gunn, of the
Medical College of New York, do not hesitate to
to recommend it, and use it in their practice : for
as Dr. Gunn says in his published book, “the
ingredients of the remedy are among the most
valuable of Materia Medica.” He also declares
that he knows the medicine is used by leading
phvsicians. but they will not let their patients
know they employ it. Mr. Hugh Frazer,
director of Frazer’s Gold Mining Co., Yilgarn,
W.A., testifies as follows Perth, West Aus-
tralia, 3rd January, 1891.—Two or three years
ago, when subjected to the privations of a mining
prospector, I was taken ill, pains invaded all parts
of my body, and kidney disease, with fever and
ague set in. I was also seized with cramps and
shivering fits, and my sufferings became so
intense that I thought I would sink under my
afflictions, and I fully believe I would have done
so, but for Warner’s safe cure which promptly
relieved the most distressing symptoms. I took
in all eight bottles of the medicine, and I can
vouch that through its agency I recovered, and
now I feel a new man, mentally and physically.

An incident that was like squash in the
desert or cold beer in a great thirsty land took
place the other day. There was a gentle little
maiden walking along alone—a bashful demoiselle
with big, wondering, dreamy eyes and dainty cos
tume, and she carried her purse in her hand after
the manner of irresponsible womanhood. Pre-
sent.y she dropped the purse, and a shiny dude
who was passing leaped to pick it up for her. He
was so quick in his movements that she didn’t
reahse her loss till she saw him with the purse in
his hand, and then she hurriedly resolved that he
was a pickpocket, and brought her parasol down
with a orash'Upon his hat. The rest is too painful
for description. By the time he managed to
explain, he was whacked in eight different places,
and the girl was hot and red and flustered, and
her calm, dreamy eyes looked as if they belonged
to a carnivorous animal rushing at raw beef. She
went away at last perspiring and indignant, and
with her mouth open, panting violently, and
peace reigned once more.— Cockney.

TO THE LADIES.
Ladies wishing to exhibit their children must, at time
of entry, clearly fill in the form provided for that pur-
pose, and which may be obtained at the Office, Exhibi
tion Building (Nicholson-street entrance).
Entries must be made, with the Secretary if possible
prior tosp.nl. on the 22nd April. For the convenience
of parents residing in the country or in adjoining
colonies, entry-forms will be posted free on application
The Committee have engaged a Special Staff of Trained
Nurses, who will see that every attention and care is
bestowed upon the children and their parents, guardians
or nurses during the Exhibition.
Certificate of registration of birth must be produced to
the judges.
The judging hi the several classes will be controlled by
a committee consisting of prominent medical gentlemen.
The awards for the Champion Prizes and Special Prizes
will be made by public vote. Every person paying for
admission to ■ the Exhibition Building will receive a
ballot-paper, upon which they can vote for the children
they consider entitled thereto. This ballot system will
be Controlled. by a committee, to be elected by the exhi-
bitors themselves.
The committee, which consists of prominent medical
specialists, intend giving lectures every afternoon and
evening to mothers on “Nursing and the General Treat-
ment of Children,” and will distribute a pamphlet for the
guidance of parents.
The limited number of entries are rapidly being
received, and early application is necessary.
Oil behalf of the Committee of Management,
T. G. HAMPTON, Secretary.

Round the Shops and Shows.
When a lady goes out for a day’s shopping
iu'-Sydney, the wisest thing she call do, when she
gets into the city, is to walk up King-street to
Mr. Quong Tart’s tea-rooms and fortify huisol
witli a enp of his matchless tea, and a deliciow s
hot buttered scone. And at one o’clock, when the
inner man again asserts himself, she cannot do better
than return and partake of the very excellent
repast provided in the shape of soups, fish, grills,
&o-, I ( io crowd of customers who frequent
. the rooms at mid-day speaks volumes, and many
are content to sit in the waiting-room for over half
an hour, watching for their chance of a seat at one
of the numerous tables. An amiable and attentive
manageress contributes in no small measure to th 0
many attractions of Quong Tart’s tea-rooms.
* * #
Messrs. Hordern Bros., of 207 Pitt-street
(only address) arc at present showing a splendid
stock of Dr. Jaeger’s celebrated woollen under-
clothing. These garments are so strongly recom-
mended by the faculty that it would ho almost
tempting Providence to wear afiVlhihg else. The
material is delightfully _ soft, and unlike most
woollen goods, it .does not irritate the skin. ’.Messrs.
Hordern Bros, are having an immense sale of
these articles, and have t!\em in both the light and
heavy makes, so as to suit everybody.
# # #
Mi’. Wyatt's Oriental Bazaar affords a
pleasant retreat to those who take an interest in
Japanese and Indian curios. In the “ cony
corner ” or “divan ” one enjoys comfortably cush-
ioned seats, and by merely looking around can
obtain all kinds of new ideas concerning the
decoration of walls, doorways, pictures, and the
like. A beautiful black Japanese wood cabinet
with panels in raised designs in gold, pearl, and
ivory, is well worth inspection—and the scores of
curios bestrewn here, there and everywhere in
the bazaar fill one with wonder. It is, as the
spider said to the fly, a place where you see so
many pretty things you never saw before.
*• * #
Mrs. McCathie, of 80 King-street, is to
the fore as usual, and has taken care to provide
her well-known establishment with a large stock
of ladies and children’s sanitary underclothing.
The children’s sleeping-suits are particularly cosy,
the material being made from the finest Alpine
wool, is both healthful and comfortable. The
combinations are made in sizes to suit all, i
from the tiniest tot upwards. It is a well-
known fact that consumption is often contracted
in consequence of insufficient chest-covering, but
surely anyone weaving, at any rate during the
winter months, the double - breasted singlets
would be able to keep all chest complaints at bay.
These wonderful woollen articles, though so light
in weight, afford the most complete protection
against all changes in the weather. Some are
minus trimming, whilst others are elaborately
ornate with torchon lace which has been dyed a
neutral tint to harmonise with the material."

Since the days of the ancients, when Flora,
the goddess of flowers, held sway, floral decora-
tions, in whatever shape or form, has been the
special province of the weaker sex. Doubtless,
then, the success of the decorations at the last
Government House ball (April 13th) was duo to
the fact that they were designed and carried out
by Mrs. Trump and her daughters, who have
lately purchased the business formerly carried on
by Maude and Co., 141, King-street. These
enterprising ladies are already doing brisk busi-
ness in the floral line.

Where there is such keen competition in
the musical-instrument business as in Sydney it
is difficult to make up one’s mind where to go for
the best piano. When, however, the importer is
himself a thorough musician, that fact alone should
be sufficient to decide one. A poor piano would
find no use in Mr. Chas. Huenerbein’s warehouse,
■3lB George-street. It is, therefore, certain that
by purchasing there no mistake could be made.
Mr. Huenerbein has just opened a special class of
pianos, with all the latest improvements, by Lipp
Nteinway, Vogel, Rubenstein, Hoff, Schmidt’

celebrated Brinsmead pianos, for which Mr.

English cost. The tuning and repairing of pianos

vision.
