Mr. Peter Thoinle has just arrived in
Sydney for the purpose of establishing an art
furniture business. The only silver medal
awarded at the Melbourne International Exhibi-
tion to Brisbane was taken by Mr. Thoinle for
industrial work. Air. Thoinle, who has taken
several medals at the various Australian exhibi-
tions, intends to patent in Sydney and introduce bis
automatic cash-ti 11 and recording machine, which
not only shows the amount of each purchase, but
i egisters it and accurately totals up the aggre-
gate. On arrival Air. Thoinle was entertained at
a ball and supper given in his honour by Herr
.nans Pasmussen at his residence in Paddington.
Blackfellow “Tom Ritchie,’’ the last
King of Orang-Oranga ” —or Aringa—the name
ot.tne tribe of happy grub-eating savages who
originally roamed over what is now the Portland
and I ort hairy country of Victoria—was buried
a la whitefellow at Condah Mission Station ceme-
tery on 15th insfc. Poor old “Tom,” who must
nave picked up his re-christening from the Ritchies
--who, in the old days, owned Aringa Station —
tell upon bad times during the eve of his life- rum
and missionary made him a white-man’s mangy
slave, with an incoherent gabble about “gor-
speltroo and _ “ ally.yooyah,” dependent on
government rations and lies for bare tucker,
instead of a knife-scarred but absolute monarch
over slayers and snarers of game. Hence,
rn-tead of being eaten, or smoke - dried in
a . h's Majesty was shoved into the
civilised deal coffin, with his title brass-plated on
the lid, though the usual lies as to his virtues
were not considered necessary to the occasion.
He seemed to be over 70, and, under missionary
turnon, had developed into an ingenious, poor-
pfeller-me, cadger. If you doubted his Royalty,
you couldn’t gainsay his genuine “gorspel,” for
he could gabble “Our Father chart never;”
tor a wager of baccy and a stiff-un, without
making more than 28 blackfellow’s mistakes. Vale,
King Tom !
The lassoo of the “Wild West” has en-
ainouml the merry ambition of Charters Towers
childhood. A knot of women gossiping on a
verandah, with two girls seated jauntily on the
verandah railing, attracted the attention the
other of four embryotic cowboys, who
were “prowling the prairies” of the Charters
lowers streets, clothes-line in hand, in search of
prey. The verandah temptation was altogether
beyond resistance. “ Whew-izz !” went the
lariat; “Whoop!” cried the urchins, and two
lassies went tumbling backwards like roped angels
turning somersaults, while four madly yelling
kiddies went rushing up-street until the necessary
jerk proclaimed that the quarry was snared and
resistant. Now came a tug of war—Indian
whoops and women’s screams, and no one knows
how the god of battles would have decided the
affair, had not a sensible little girl sneaked into
the house, returned with a carving knife and cut
the Gordian rope, sending both parties of tuggers
all a-sprawl in reverse directions. That girl must
be Joan of Arc, whom a legend says never dies in
the flesh.

conceded to be the most effective of all blood-
purifiers.

CAN GANGER BE GORED?
COUNT MATTEI CHALLENGES THE
DOCTORS.
A very interesting article appears in the January
number of the Review of Reviews on the subject
of one of the most terrible of all diseases, ’‘Can-
cer.” The medical Faculty ha e hitherto plainly
declared it to be “incurable,” What a horrible
significance that word has ! Count Mattel, an
Italian nobleman, claims to have cured cancer not
in one but in many instances. He is a rich man
who began a course of scientific study at middle
age, and the first clue to his alleged wonderful
medicine is said to have been arrived at by watch-
ing a sheepdog afflicted with a virulent attack of
mange. This dog absented itself daily, and
curiosity led Mattel to follow it: when ho found
that it made for the mountain and there scratched
up and ate a particular r dt.
Mr. Stead, the editor of the Review of Reviews,
heard so much from persons of note as to (In'
efficacy of-Mattel’s treatment, that in the public
interest he went over to Bolonga to interview the
count, and relates the result of his researches.
Should Count Matters specific prove to be all
that is claimed for it, it will no doubt become as
great a public boon as the famous St. Jacobs Oil,
which has effected so many marvellous cures in
cases of nervous affections, such as rheumatism,
neuralgia, muscular contusions, &c. St. Jacobs
Oil has become a household word throughout the
civilised world, and has been the means of rescuing
thousands from pain and misery. All who have
tried it agree that it is a valuable domestic remedy
that should have a place in every well-regulated
household.

Love-lays of a Lunatic.
I traced, on the firm, smooth, silver sand,
Four words, with fevered, hasty hand —
“ Dulce, I love thee!
Just then, in rolled an envious sea
And swept away both words and me.
(Dulce, I love thee !)
Oh, ci'uel wave ! oh, fickle shore !
In thy domains I’ll write no more
“ Dulce, I love thee !”
“ I’ll stand on Himalaya’s peak
And, with a giant’s strength, I’ll tweak
Some blackened pine-tree from the root,
And write across the heavens, in soot,
“Dulce, I love thee !”
[The Bulletin advises this man not to write
poetry to his girl in future: it’s safer and much
more explicit to wink at her.]

I Dr. M. PERRY,
1 M.E.C.S., L.y A , &c.,
SPECIALIST for the Treatment of Nervous
Complaints, Diabetes, Tumors, &c.
Registered by the Medical Bo tr lof N.S. W. Lite House Sur-
geon to the Great Northern Hospital. London
I am aware that I am not strictlv following the paths
Of professions! etiquette by advertising myself as a
Specialist for the eatin~nt of this class of disease, but,
unfortunately for the suffering public in Australia the
apathy shown by Qualified Men in the treatment of
Chronic and Nervous Complaints has induced men of the
lowest grade of intellect (whose only qualifications are
unlimited assurance) to spring up, and by specious
advertisements and false promises trap the unwary.
It is very evident that sufferers from these diseases
have been scandalously treated by these unqualified
persons The evidence taken at the Medical Enquiry
held in Sydney (published in the Daily Telegraph, Nov.
6th 7th and Bth. IS9U) plainly shows the disgraceful
manner in which patients are duped. Not only are
they robbed of their money hut what is of infinitely
greater importance, their health is injured also. It is
therefore time that Legally Qualified Men entered the
field to fight these pests of society with their own
weapons, viz., Advertising.
It is utterly impossible for those who are suffering
from the e diseases who are scattered over 11 parts of
Australasia, to ascertain which -Qualified Practitioner
makes these diseases his speciality unless he advertises.
I consider therefore, that by advertising I not n ly
strike a blow at the Quackery which now exists, but J also add
to the usefulness of my profession and in no way lessen viy
personal dignity.
lUt Qualifications I Invito the fullest Investigation Into. For some considerable time I was House Surgeon io the
Great Northern Hospital, London, whei e T had special opportunities of c servin the many phases of the Nervous a.d
Sexua .system, and to be ome a-cquain'ed with the most scientific method* oi tnectlog their cure.
My sole attention is given to the treatment of these diseases, an ) when a person consults me he is informed at he
commencement whether I can effect his cure or not. The patient is not deluded with false and specious promises, and
if I undertake the case
I Absolutely Guarantee a Complete anti Permanent Cure.
My great success in the treatment of these diseases enables me to give this guarantee without fear.

Tales of the Old Regime.
No. VI.—The Drowning of Captain Bankes.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
On the disembarkation of the passengers
(Convicts Jane Applegarth 23, Minnie Reade 18,
Fanny Cockburn 21, and Julia Perry 26) from
that trip of the Cyprus, the last stage of which
had been so nearly marked by a catastrophe to
Captain Bankes, Commandant of Macquarie
Harbour,* who had been thrown overboard by
the strong-willed and strong-armed Applegarth
girl, Bankes, drenched, hurried to change his
clothing. Thus he gave Skipper Jobson a brief
opportunity to communicate with Applegarth,
which he did in a whisper.
"Hold up, my plucky un, I’ll help you all I
can. Ido like pluck when I sees it!”
The girl, handcuffed, moved impulsively her
braceleted hands as though to thank him for his
re-assuring words, but the steel clips reminded
her that even so slight a gesture as a handshake
was now impossible. She burst into tears, and it
seemed as thougn that native spirit of resolution
which had nerved her, in defence of her own
and her sister-transports’ honour, to immerse
Officer—and Gentleman —Bankes, had at last
vanished under the galling ignominy of her
manacles. Her head fell upon her breast, which
surged with the violence of her grief and her fear.
Bankes had promised when she had asked him
tauntingly why he did not flog her and brand her,
that he would do the one and the other, and she
would have been more than woman—more, indeed,
than human—if, hearing his wrathful tones and
seeing the infuriated gleam in his eyes, her flesh
had not crept in anticipation of the body-torturing,
soul-degrading punishment which she knew would
follow.
* • •
Noting her tears, Jobson telt “ bad,” and
though he was pretty high up in the System—
and was quite entitled to entertain the hope of
attaining still greater distinction in its service
—he came to what, all things considered, was a
very remarkable resolve. His interest coincided
with his duty, which was to throw no impediment
in the way, if not to facilitate the administration
by the Commandant, of punishment to so dread-
ful an offender as Jane Applegarth had proved
herself, and to depart from duty was to prejudice
his interests. And here let us say, to those of our
present readers who have not read the narrative
of Captain Bankes’ negotiation with that other
excellent officer of the System, James Fubster,
Require, Principal Superintendent of Convicts in
Hobart Town, on the successful issue of which
Captain Bankes had become possessed of the
bodies —the men of the Regime never inven-
toried souls—of the four girls; that the pro-
vocation which induced Applegarth to commit
the crime was no justification for it. You
can only appreciate to the full extent of its
heinousness the significance of such a deed as the
girl’s when we state it was tantamount to, if not
more infamous than, high treason. If, at that
time, British popular opinion—no, we apologise
to the people, and say, the opinion of the rulers of
Britain—held it permissible to draw on a hurdle,
to hang, to disembowel while life was still in the
body, and to quarter the palpitating carcase of a
criminal convicted of offences against the sacred
person'of British Majesty, there was all the more
reason to punish with the utmost rigour of the
Law, and the Regulations which were higher than
the Law, a person who could assault Captain
Bankes.
This is an absurd statement, you may say. Let
us reason it out. It was admitted by a Minister
in Parliament, t speaking of men like Bankes,
that the Commandant of Macquarie Harbour was
necessarily and properly endowed with a power
and an authority touching the lives of “ felons ”
which could not be exercised by the Sovereign
himself or any other person within the bounds of
the United Kingdom. If, then, the sanctity of
the divinity that did hedge so potential a person-
age had been rudely assailed, it were surely
open to him to assert his dignity by pun-
ishments unknown to British Law, but, neverthe-
less, tolerated by the System. It were nothing but
right for the men of the Regime to put the System
above the Law, and as a Penal Commandant was
admitted by a Secretary of State to be more
powerful than the Sovereign, then the penalties
for outraging the Commandant’s person should
be, if not novel, surpassingly awful. Therefore,
let Captain Bankes be justified.
A further word. Let Jane Applegarth weep as
she might ; let her flinch under the heated iron
and shrink from the barber’s shears; let her
writhe, as the scourger who had never wavered
when he gave this man five hundred lashes, and
that man three hundred, and a third a thousand,
all in one day—we can place our fingers on the
record—as that scourger flicked faint-heartedly his
cat upon her gleaming shoulders; let her in
uncouth phrase express between clenched teeth
her upbraidings of destiny, we must still say :
“ Well, well, she surely deserved it all! ” If we
don’t say so, then we must declare that it is a
toss-up which of four, Bankes, Commandant of the
Harbour, Sorell, Lieutenant-Governor of Hobart
Town, Brisbane, Governor-General in Sydney,
and Bathurst, Minister for the Colonies, in Lon-
don, was the greater scoundrel. For all knew,
the greatest man as well as the smallest, that
Officers—and Gentlemen—shaved—and flogged—
and branded—women.
* * *
Colonel Foveaux, of chaste and honoured
memory, was, in old colonial days, generally
credited with being the author of the method
which branded men built in the image of their
Creator with red-hot irons. This may be a libel
upon that representative man of the Regime, for
the invention of, even a set of branding-
irons required an ingenious faculty of which
we can find no trace in the history of
his unique personality. The chances are, look-
ing at the plain experience of the world, which
teaches that the big man always gets the credit
for owning the little man’s brains, that it was
some originally-transported underling who had
conceived the idea of an appliance which should
indelibly stamp mortal flesh. But whoever was
the originator, mattered not. Lieutenant Darrell,
, Bankes’ immediate subordinate, who had invented
the plan of marooning on the Grummet Rock and
also the pear-gag, J and who was rather a con-
noisseur in forms of torture, had introduced it on
Sarah Island. The Settlement set of irons was
not perfect alphabetically, for the labour available
in the smithy of the Harbour lacked the requisite
capacity to fashion the whole of the characters,
but Darrell, with that superfine ingenuity of his,
had devised five irons of the following patterns :
\ 0 I I =>
By combining these, sometimes with the handle
up and sometimes down, any desirable symbol
could be affixed to a convict’s body. You may,
perhaps, point out that anyone could have planned
so simple an appliance, but such a conclusion on
your part, O reader, would be hasty, and due to
the fact that you cannot fathom the necessities of
the System. Darrell could ; he did not believe in
minimising punishment, and therefore what you
regard as so trifling a device, he knew would meet
with the chiefest requirement of the penal routine.
That’s where this superfine quality came in; as
you will see.
• • •
But we must get on.
Officer—and Gentleman —Bankes, having put
himself into dry clothes, within an hour of the
landing of the Cyprus’ cargo (23 prisoners) and
passengers (4), constituted himself a Court of
Justice in which to try (and condemn, for that
was a foregone conclusion) Jane Applegarth, aged
23, per Duke of Wellington, No. 513, who had
been transported originally for seven years, and
who had been consigned to Macquarie Harbour
nominally as a private soldier’s wife, but, actually
and without her knowledge or consent, to serve as
one of four concubines for the Commandant.
As Commandant Bankes he presided, as Cap-
tain Bankes he prosecuted, as Bankes, the insulted
and half-drowned gentleman, he gave evidence
for the prosecution. He was in full-dress, as he
sat in No. 1 school-room, which was the West-
minster Hall of Sarah Island, but, as a matter of
comfort, he unhooked his sword and let it rest upon
the Bible. When Backhouse and Walker visited
the Harbour, nine years later, they regretted that
there was not an ampler supply of Bibles, but
they were assured that the Settlement population
had never felt any particular discomfort on
account of the small number of copies of the book
on the Island. “You see, sirs, ” had said Major
Bailee, “ there was always the Commandant’s
copy ! ”
Upon the Bible rested the sword, and the im-
plement of authority which was to be wielded by
Justice was thus sanctified. All that was wanted
to perfect the arrangement, was a blazon of the
Royal Arms and a “G.R.” These were missing.
A guard of ten soldiers,, however, did something
to atone for the deficiency.
“Call Jane Applegarth,” ordered the Bench —
and the girl was ushered in. Her face was tear-
stained, though her eyes were dry. She shivered,
but it might have been from cold; it was not
necessarily fear. Except the guard and the
Sergeant, the Judge and the prisoner, who were
also the accused and the accuser, were alone.
Jobson, Bowser, the skipper and mate of the
schooner Cyprus, who had seen the assault upon
the high and reverend person of the Commandant,
were in the vicinity of the Court, as were also the
three other girls, but none was at present per-
mitted access to the scene where Justice was
about to revel.
“Jane Applegarth, I charge you with having
assaulted an officer m His Majesty’s forces while
engaged in the performance of his duty as Com-
mandant of the Penal Establishment situate at
Macquarie Harbour within His Majesty’s Colony
of Yan Dieman’s Land, whereby his life was
endangered and a breach of good discipline com-
mitted. What do you say— ‘ Guiltv or Not
Guilty ’?”
“Who was the of’cer?” said the girl, speaking
very civilly.
“The officer? Why, of course, I was the
officer ! Are you guilty or not guilty ?”
“Were ye engaged in the performance of yer
dooty when I ducked ye ?”
“ Guilty or Not Guilty ?” reiterated the Justice.
“ I won’t answer till ye answer me !” Then she
turned to the military guard, and though Bankes
' ainly strove io stop the torrent of her words, she
thus addressed them :
“Look here, sodgers, be ye men? If ye are,
and ye ever remember yer mother or yer sisters,
or yer sweethearts, yell never let me be punished
for a-throwin’ of that scoundrel into the sea. I
can’t tell you men what he called me an’ t’other
girls, who came with me, or what he told us he
wanted us for. I ain’t a of’cer an’ I ain’t a gen’l’-
man, an’ so I can’t say or think such things. But
I can tell you this—he said as he was agoin’ to
shave me, and flog me, and brand me, an’ to show
he meant it, see what he put on me !” (She held
up her handcuffed hands.) “ See them,” she went
on, with a great scorn struggling for utterance
against the lump which come into her woman's
throat. “See them! See the bright things he
puts upon a girl to show that he means to do the
other things ! But I’d sooner wear these than do
what he wants us to do !”
She stopped for a second. Bankes mumbled
something; she took no heed, but dropping her
voice to a bitter satiric whisper, that should have
pricked the consciences of the guard as the steel-
points of their own bayonets were wont to prick
the flesh of rebellious male transports, she re-
peated her enquiry, “ Be ye men ? ”
She waited for a reply. None came ; the men
rested at ease on their muskets. Then—
She turned her back upon them, and spoke to
Bankes : “Go along, Cap’n—do yer best. If the
chaps of my own sort won’t help me, if they won’t
shoot ye where ye sit, yer not to blame, it’s them.
I’m no scholard, but I knows this—there would
be no devils like you, if there were no men like
them to take the devil s pay.”
* # #
Bankes called in Jobson, Bowser, and the
girls Cockburn, Reade and Perry.
“ Captain Jobson, will you tell the court ? ” said
the Judge-cum-Prosecutor, “what you saw of this
assault upon me.”
The moment had come for Jobson to act upon
the resolve which very injudiciously in his own
interests he had framed. This was that he would
foil Officer—ai d Gentleman—Bankes.
“I didn’t see nothing of the assault,” he
asserted, bluffly, “ I was a-lookin’ after my
vessel. ”
“ Jobson !” exclaimed the astonished Com-
mandant, “Jobson !”
“That’s what I say.”
“ I’ll swear you, sir,” said Bankes. Handing
fSee “Tales of the System” (No. 20)—Lieut. Darrell’s
Predicament.
the Bible to the Skipper—his sword fell with a
clank to the floor through his hurried movement —
Bankes put Jobson on his oath to tell the truth,
the whole truth, and nothing but the truth.
Now Jobson had seen the whole affair. Bankes,
having made openly what amounted to an in-
famous proposal to the four girls, as they
stood on the vessel’s deck, and having
been met by a sharp rebuff from Applegarth,
had proceeded to arrest and handcuff her
with his own white and rigid hands. Through
the lurching of the vessel occasioned by Jobson’s
own instructions to the steersman, the Captain
fell while performing his exalted duty, and Apple-
garth had taken advantage of his plight to trans-
fer him from the vessel’s deck to the briny billows.
Jobson, we repeat, had seen the whole thing, but,
nevertheless, on oath persisted that he had ob-
served nothing.
“ I’ll commit you for perjury,” said Bankes.
c “Do so, at your peril,” said the other man.
“ Don’t you forget, sir, that I’m a free man. You
can’t play fast and loose with free men like me.”
But, nevertheless, the schooner Captain was
hustled out of Court by some of the guard, throw-
ing, as he went out, a final word of encouragement
to the poor prisoner. “ Keep up heart, Sis, keep
up heart.”
Mr. iiowser was by no means so recalcitrant a
witness for the prosecution as his superior officer.
Whether it was that he had a greater regard for
his oath, or whether it was that he felt that
Jobson’s conduct had placed within his grasp, the
mastership of the Government schooner, we may
not say, but he very circumstantially deposed to
the brutal, diabolical attempt upon the life of
that venerated functionary who presided over that
Court.
...iy questions to ask the witness?” queried
the Bench.
“Did you not hear that man say . . . ?”
“No,” said Mr. Bowser, “no, I don’t rec’lect.”
“ Do you rec’lect on the passage down atellin’
me an’ the girls that Cap’n Bankes was an of’cer
and a genTman? Ye said he was a perfect
genTman, didn’t ye?
“I rec’lect that,’* said Bowser, making a most
brilliant bid for the Skippership. “I speaks of
pe’ple as I finds ’em. I alius found him a puffeck
genTman! ”
“ Is it a perfect genTraan’s conduct to lay traps
for a poor girl, to deceive poor girls- ay, an’ not
one noor girl alone, but four? Is that yer gent’l’-
man?” 8
ocand down, witness,” said the Bench.
If Bowser answered, history failed to catch his
accents, overborne as they were by the voice of
one who seemed to have modelled his excellence
upon that of the First Gentleman in Europe.
• * *
“ Call Lie girl Perry !” Julia Perry stood
forward and kissed the book, but Jobson’s talk
with her outside had materially operated upon
her memory. “She couldn’t rec’leet nothin’”
either. Equally forgetful was Minnie Reade.
She, too, persisted, in spite of threats from the
Bench, in saying that she knew nothing.
“I will treat you as an unwilling witness,”
declared the Bench, “ unless you tell the truth.”
She remained firm. Nevertheless, Bankes was
not to be beaten.
“ I’ll send you back to Hobart Town at once,”
he said, “in irons—mark you, girl, in irons,
unless you tell the truth.”
The_ quiet-looking lass, for answer, retorted :
“I wish you would, sir. I’d get a letter away
then to Mrs. Ery.”§
Applegai th’s interests considered, the response
was a very unfortunate one, for it put the Com-
mandant on his mettle. Accustomed to the exer-
cise of absolute power and arbitrary jurisdiction,
Bankes, though in his cooler moments he was not
indifferent to the possibilities of offending the
flaccid public opinion of Hobart Town, could not
brook so direct a threat, and the girl’s speech,
kindly conceived as it was, simply brought down
upon her his wrath.
“I’ll charge you with being an accessory,” he
said, “ unless you tell the truth.”
The girl would possibly have stuck to her denial
but the prisoner interposed.
“Say ye saw it, Minnie, say ye saw it! He
has made up his mind to punish me an’ nothin’ ye
can do will stop him.”
And so Minnie Reade, 18, per Duke of Welling-
ton, No. 54G, testified that she had witnessed the
assault. Now, of course, she had committed per-
jury, but, singularly enousrh, the Judge said no-
thing on that point—doubtless because he had
gained what he wanted.
“Have you anything to ask the witness,
pris’ner ?”
“No,” she answered, “ ’cept not to furget when
I’m away from her what I told her about you.”
Pretty Fanny Cockburn when called upon for
her testimony, was not prepared to support her
fellow-transport’s view of Captain Bankes’ con-
duct. On her first introduction to the Commandant,
she had, as you will remember, bestowed a coy and
coquettish glance on him, which had not been
without something of an effect upon Bankes’ heart
—as susceptible to a pretty woman’s glance, as it
was hardened to the touch of outraged justice, and
Cockburn, after careful consideration with herself,
had come to the conclusion that if the other girls
were going to be virtuous fools, she at least would
not. She did not, in short, see why she should
not be permanently installed in Officers’Quarters.
We may here anticipate a little, we give you the
assurance on the authority of the Rev. J. A.
Manton, first official chaplain to the Harbour, that
she was still there when, many years subsequently,
he visited the Settlement. Captain Bankes had
long been dead-drowned by the hands of Jane
Applegarth—other Commandants had come, and
other Commandants had gone, but still pretty,
frail Fanny Cockburn had remained on. Even
when Parson Mam on remonstrated she ex-
pressed her intention to continue as the current
Commandant’s “ guest ’’—and meek, unconscious
dove !—“ She could not see any particular harm in
so doing.” Neither could the Commandant !
She saw no particular harm, either, in answer-
ing the very leading questions which Com-
mandant-Justice Bankes put to her. We will not
recapitulate them ; that would be unnecessary, as
you will form some idea, from what has gone
before, of the method in which he secured her
testimony. What we are concerned with is the
influence of her evidence upon the poor prisoner,
Jane could hardly believe her ears, as she heard
the witness, not merely depose to the facts, but
go far beyond them in her eagerness to propitiate
the uniformed deity of the Harbour, for her simple
nature had clung to Cockburn’s as rude and un-
couth ones so often do to delicate and super-
ficially-refined characters. In the long voyage
from “Home,” in the factory at Hobart, and on
the three weeks’ passage from the Capital to the
Harbour, she had thrown, as it were, her own
personality around Cockburn as a protecting
mantle. Her love for the girl had held her bond-
woman more closely than the System had done,
and her attitude towards Bankes on the declara-
tion of his infamous intentions towards her in
particular, and all the girls in general, had been
prompted more by solicitude for Cockburn’*
purity than by any regard for herself, or Perry,
or Reade. Thus, to have her affection thrown
back upon herself, was to hurt her bitterly,
and to have it made apparent te her i*
addition that Fanny was willing to re-
sign herself to the arms of Officer —and gentle-
man and satyr—Bankes, crushed her in very
agony. The vase of her tears seemed broken, so
fierce was their outburst; and she flung herself
upon the floor of the Court and there lay prostrate,
refusing to be comforted by Perry, who, un-
checked, bent over and bade her “ never mind.”
• » *
We will, to-day, pass over the shaving
scene, the flogging episode, and the branding.
We may not surfeit you - horrors, like sweets,
should be dished up at intervals. But we will
take you to Limeburners’ Point whence, a week
after her experience of the Court, Jane Apple-
garth, almost bald, and with a festering letter
“ W ” upon her shoulders, which had been placed
artistically above the stripe marks that Officer—
and Gentleman —Bankes had imprinted upon the
smooth, white surface—not, of course, by his own
hands, but through the medium of a scourger—
was removed under military guard. Limeburners’
Point, at this time, was populated by five good-
conduct transports, and one convict-overseer, who
all lived—that is to say, dined, supped, and slept
—in one hut, built partly of dried mud, partly of
acacia-stems.
x.iin uiu, perhaps sixteen by fourteen, was the
solitary tenement at Limeburners Point.
And yet Officer—and Gentleman—Bankes sent
Jane Applegarth, woman-transport, there!
• * #
She had been there a week whem the
pilot-boat, which brought the lime-burning gang
its weekly supply of rations, ran up to the beach.
Jobson was on board ; he was under suspension,
and had been removed by Bankes’ fiat from the
skippership, but being a “free on arrival ” man,
Bankes had not dared to imprison him. He had,
however, been confined to the pilot station till the
schooner Cyprus was in readiness to return. The
bribe of a pipeful, all round the crew, of tobacco
had induced the pilot to promise to take Jobson
with him on his next ration-trip. One of the
things the great men of the System were most
reluctant to learn was, that not one subordinate
in a hundred could be depended upon to resist a
bribe—in which, of course, the subordinates only
followed the example of the great men themselves.
Jobson was the first out of the boat. He forced
his way over the shingle, covered thickly, as it
was, with decomposing and noisome kelp, upon
which the lime gang had more than once been
compelled to exist when stormy weather —or
official forgetfulness—had prevented the pilot-
boat bringing the rations, and hurried to the hut.
There he found Jane —Heavens, how he found
her!
“Poor girl! Poor girl,” he said, with
the tear-drops coursing down the ridges rough-
sea-weather of varied latitudes had scored in his
cheeks ; “ I wish I’d killed him, and by G
I’ll do it yet ! ”
“ No,” said Jane, “ don’t do that ! ” Jane was
not crying—she said then—and not for many years
did her experience contradict her words—that she
would never cry again.
“ What ! ” cried, almost shouted, Jobson,
“what, would you let that man go, after what he
has done? I ’udn’t think so much of yer, Janie,
if yer couldn’t hate him with—with ” The
crust of this man’s nature, which had been formed
during long years of acquaintance with the Sys-
tem, had been penetrated by the mystic shaft of
that reverence of the man for the woman, which
has at once been the curse and the salvation of
the race. He, whom the groans of a thousand men
had failed to move, at the sigh, and the accent of
suffering of one for whom he cared, had stripped
himself of place, of pay, and of authority, and
had now gone to the opposite pole of opinion. To
him the Regime, and its instruments were now
abhorrent.
“ With wot ? ” the woman asked.
“ With the hate I have now ! ” —the pregnant
volume of that word !—“ that I now bear the
S’tem !”
“ ’Ate ’im,” she said with a flash of her old fire,
“ ’ate ’im ; I ’ate ’im so much, that I’d give myself
to ’im, so that at the very moment ’is lips touched
mine, I cud stab ’im to the ’eart!”
Her w rds, and the intensity of the passion
which rent her, astounded Jobson, but gratified
him. They did another thing, too—they inspired
him with an idea, which he communicated to her.
In very halting phrase he spoke, for the man had
learnt to strongly love her, even in her shame and
her desolation, and he had invested her with that
sacredness which the true lover, from Dante and
Petrarch to the Cully of the slum Lais, ever in-
vests his mistress, and he was fearful of violating
it, even by a verbal suggestion.
“Send to him,” he said, “and tell him it will
be all right. But ask him to come here, as a
favour to you, because—you ain’t got anything to
wear ’mong the other girls. I know something of
Bankes—him, though I couldn’t ha’ thought he’d
have done this to a woman !- and, I believe, he’ll
come. And I’ll be here, too —you get him to come
out round the Point—and I’ll be there, hard by
the ti-tree—and then—l’ll—finish him.”
The girl pondered, flushed, and, then, of a
sudden, paled.
“Oh, Cap’n Jobson, y e are a good ’un, but ye
won’t do that fur me, for I won’t let ye, I’mae'oin’
to kill him myself if I get the chance. ’Sides,
ye’d be hanged—it don’t matter wot ’appens to
me, but I ain’t a-goin’ to let you be swung on my
’count, ’’
“No, lass,” said Jobson. “I won’t be swung,
nor you neither. The pilot-boat men don't love
Bankes, nor the lime-gang, and then, after I’ve—
we’ve—finished him, ttie cutter’ll take me to the
schooner, and once aboard and Bankes dead, I’ll
show Bowser who’s skipper. I’ll run down here
with her then, put you on board, and get you off.
If the doc. sends any despatches along, on my
soul they’ll never reach Hobart Town !”
But she would not consent. And then, at last,
he told her that he would do the honest thing by
her—he would marry her—if she would assist him
in the realisation of his scheme. Even then, with
the Paradise of the convict woman flung open
before her, she would not promise, but, finally,
they struck the happy mean of a compromise.
She should drown Bankes, if she could she had
thrown him into the sea once, and she wished to
throw him in a second time in such a way that he
would not come out—alive. Had she not, indeed,
told the Commandant so on his first defining his
intention to appropriate the girls? And if she
could not drown Bankes, if he were too strong for
her, then and then only was Jobson to come to
her assistance. Between the two, they could
scarcely doubt their success, if Bankes would only
Come to the Point. And after, well—
“ Wife or pot, Jobson, I’ll go with ye to the
world’s end an’ be true to ye till death. Strike

“Adam Bede,” c/o Abbott,
Post Oifice Chambers, SYDNEY, N.s. W.
me, ’ate me, or kill me, I’ll alius be faithful to ye,
Jobson, an’ never grumble wotever ye do.”
* # •
Officer—and Gentleman —Bankes (we will
not let you forget, that Bankes was a Gentleman
by birth and his Majesty’s Commission) tumbled j
into the trap. When the pilot coxswain com-
municated Jane’s message —that she wanted to
•ee him, to beg his pardon, and to do what he
wanted, and would promise never to interfere
between him again and any women—he was not
above experiencing the fierce joy which is sup-
posed to be the privilege of a man —and him only
—who meets a worthy foeman. Bankes had been
worsted for a time, and, therefore, this act of sub-
mission by Jane Applegarth was like incense to
his nostrils.
The day after the receipt of the message, he
attired himself in neat undress and made the
trip. He had actually some thought of over-
aweing the Pointers in full, military fig, with red
coat and shako, and pipeclay belts, and clanking
sword, and was only dissuaded from his intention
by the reflection that the splashing spray on the
trip down and up would stain the first and rust
the last. When he went up again, the question
of uniform did not interest him so much.
With craven abjectness of attitude and gesture,
the lime-burners and their overseer welcomed
him. And after a formal inspection of them,
and the making of certain official inquiries as to
their welfare—there was a regulation signed by
Governor Macquarie which ordered that all Com-
mandants, in visiting outlying stations, should
inquire as to the welfare of the convicts, “their
sleeping accommodation and their rations ” (the
whole farce of examination might have occupied
twenty seconds) —he demanded the presence of
the woman.
“ Oom’dant,” grinned the overseer, “she is
modest, she is ! She don’t a-like to come out, sez
she, an’ see yer Honor, with other men bout, as
she ain’t got any togs, so to speak, sir. Shedidn t
come down well purwided, sir, not by no means,
an’ things ’a been a leetle bit rough ere, sir.”
Where is she?” demanded Bankes. “Where
is she?” _
‘ ’ She is in the cave, sir, round the point. She
’ud like to see you in private, sir.”
Bankes went round the Point, crushing with his
manly step the pebbles and the rotti g seaweed.
He went so eagerly, you would have tliought it
had been an assignation with a duchess, but, to
his fired imagination, it mattered not who the
woman was, it was still a woman who had
resisted him, who had defied him, but was now
about to throw herself at his feet and into his
arms. He did not remember —possibly, it would
have been all the same had he remembered —that
Applegarth had been made the sport of the con-
victs of the lime-gang. Under his isolated circum-
stances, tastidiousness in amours would have been
a vice.
She stood erect, leaning against a pillar of
rock that had been honeycombed by the fret of
the wave. In the gusty wind which broke against
the Point ere it howled eddying the spray into the
harbour, the one outer garment which he and the
other scoundrels and satyrs had left her was
driven in upon her form, and the outlines of a
figure, which, had it Ven decorated by the fingers
of a London costumiere and been surrounded by
the ornate splendour of a drawing-room, would
have been termed statuesque, struck his artistic
eye and inflamed his sensual passion. He looked
at her face, the newly-grafted hardness and fresh-
dinted wrinkles of which she was trying to soften
—ah, so piteously !—into a coy attractiveness,
and smiled—smiled the smile of a rake who had
been triumphant in his evil.
“ Ha, ha —this you, Jane ! You have come to
your senses, have you? You see it is no use
fighting with me ? Had you only done what I
wanted before, and kept your mouth shut, you
would not have been the recipient of—ha, ha !
the delicate attentions —he, he!—of those —ha,
ha !—gentlemen round the corner!”
Jane looked down, her breast heaved —he
thought it was with compunction, or fear, or
desire —and she twined her hands convulsively, as
■he exclaimed :
“ Y—e—es, sir ! ”
“Ah, well, my dear, we will let byegones be
byegones now. You’ve been a fool, Jeanie, but
you will be wiser in the future ”—here he laid his
hand on her shoulder—“and a deuce sight more
comfortable you’ll be up in my rooms, than in the
men’s hut yonder. What’s that?” It was a
•light rustle in the neighbouring scrub which had
caught his ear. How could he guess how near
Jobson was to breaking his promise—not to in-
terfere.
“ Nothing ! ” said the girl. She moved her
head negatively—to the face glowering in the
“ Come! ” he placed the other hand on the other
shoulder, as he spoke; “ give me a kiss then, my
girl, and say we’ll be friends ! ”
Save for a gurgled “ Oh, God ! ” a second later,
those were Commandant Bankes’ last words. The
contact of his embrace gave Applegarth the
opportunity she had been wishing for, and with one
impetuous gesture she had her thumbs into his
throat on either side of his wind-pipe, and, crook-
ing her ancle under his, threw him backwards, and
herself upon him. His head struck the shingle.
The fall had not rendered him senseless, and,
even then, Jane might not have achieved her
dreadful object—Bankes, despite his vices, had
•till something left of the well-knit frame of the
disciplined soldier—but for a sudden parting of
the ti-tree— and the fall of a sapling’s butt upon
the doomed wretch’s forehead.
The blow stunned him, and he was powerless in
the girl’s grasp. Never freeing her hands from his
throat, by a forward movement she forced his
head into the lapping waves, and there, in less
than six inches of water, she held him till a vib-
rating shudder told her that there was no occasion
to hold him longer, and that her honour and her
00-mates’ honour had been avenged.
m * *
Jane Applegarth was not hanged, though
how she freed herself from Bankes’ embrace
became known, by-and-bye, to the Doctor, who
was Acting-Commandant, vice Bankes, deceased.
And when he did hear of it, Jane was Mrs. Job-
son, and over the hills, and seas, and far away.
PRICK WARUNG.
The Bragtown Volunteers.
(A Playful Ditty, sung in a N. W.Q. Strike Camp)
It’s grand to be a Soger when
There’s nothing else to do
But point your gun at unarmed men
And draw your bally screw.
Watch how the Bragtown Volunteers
Responded, one and all,
And bade a long farewell to beers—
All at the bugle’s call.
■Echo Chords. —But tell the Scabs and Squatters, BUI,
The Sogers, and the rest,
That we’re alive and kicking still—
The Bushmen of the West.
Their Capting stepped in front, Bold Bunk,
And to his men did say :
“ My boys, I glory in your pluck;
You’re Queensland’s prop and stay.
We’ll give that noisy shearer push
A taste of martial show ;
There’s little danger of a brush,
Or, else-—/ wouldn’t go !”
Echo Chords.
“We lads can skite with any man
That ever cut a throat,
Or foraged in the far Soudan
And cotched an Ass and Goat!
When we return, the girls will rush
To mash and give us grub,
And medals bright, and lots of lush,
And tick at every pub. ”
Echo Chords.
“ But, ere we start, we’ll here agree
To, one and all, be true ;
If you will always stand by me,
I’ll always stand by you.
But, should some fluke bring on a fight,
And funk upset your mind,
I ask this favour as my right—
Please, don’t leave me behind /”
Echo Chorus. —But, tell the Scabs and Squatters, Bill,
The Sogers and the rest.
That we’re alive and kicking still-
The Bushmen of the West.

The Whirligig 1 of Time.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
I crossed the old ford at the end of last May,
The old pub. had vanished, not even a shingle
Survived of the roof, which, in years passed
away,
Saw friendship and devilry strangely com-
mingle.
The few blackened wall-posts and panels of
fencing
That stand by the roadside, are all that
remain
To tell of old days ; a new era commencing
Has ended a gold-time we’ll ne’er see again.
Then Kate was the barmaid, as handsome a girl
As any fine lady; the smile that she gave
Had put heaps of poor fellows’ heads in a whirl,
And some poor unfortunates’ bones in the
grave.
She was worshipped, you see, by the coves all
about,
Who would fight like the devil her love to
secure;
AaA Cupid, in those days, to settle a doubt,
Sad a way of his own which was certain and
sure.
And Kate! why she’d drive one insane with
her sighs,
With her pearly-white teeth and her lips red
as coral—
Sha lacked like a witch, in the depths of whose
•yes
'ft* light sparkled best at the sight of a
quarrel.
I’ll always remember that Michaelmas morning,
When, down from the Bogong, young Archie
Mackay
Stow! joking with Kate, when, without the
least warning.
Flash Jim interfered in his coarse-speaking
way.
From Archie’s warm heart to the roots of his
hair
r JPb« fiery blood rushed, with a leap and a
bound ;
Like a flash he stood off, then a blow planted
square,
Sent Jim with a thud and an oath to the
ground.
With some terrible threats the two men closed
together,
A clinch and a struggle —Flash Jim did the
rest,
For, snatching his knife from its sheathing of
leather,
With Archie’s throat gripped, drove it into
his breast.
Flash Jim was the “ringer” of Moorabin shed,
And a bit of a bully—they hated him, all—
The crowd tried to rush him—he cowed them
instead
And stood panting at bay, with his back to
the wall;
They wavered an instant, “Stand back, or by
God,” he
Exclaimed, “ who comes near me comes
straight to his death.
I ll bury the blade of this knife in his body ;
So damn you, stand off!—let a cove get his
breath !”
Then he sped through the doorway and made
for the creek,
And the crowd with a shout followed closely
behind;
Lithe-limbed and lean-flanked, Jim could stay
for a week,
A real even-timer, he sped like the wind.
They chased him through timber, to where the
tall pines
lioee out of the sandhills, set close as a furze;
Right on to the range, where the setting sun
shines
In a glory of crimson o’er ridges and spurs.
They lost him sometimes, till a stir of the
branches
Showed where he was threading the bracken
and fern,
And they followed like sleuth-hounds the trail
on his haunches—
Each man as a bushman had nothing to
learn—
So hard on his track pressed the resolute band,
impelled by a mixture of justice and passion ;
And the flying man knew that lynch-law out of
hand,
W ould follow his capture in summary fashion.
Archie’s mate, who throughout with a dogged
persistence
Had followed, stopped short, and without
much delay,
As he saw the tall ferns gently moved at a
distance
Not greater than ten or twelve paces away
With the skill that a sharpshooter picks off a
foe.
Ilia revolver discharged—ere the smoke-cloud
departed,
A body rolled down through the brushwood
below,
Some twenty odd yards from the spot where
it started.
The flash, the report, its wild echoes resounding,
Fast summoned the crowd who, with ringing
“ halloo,”
Scrambled down to the creek, where, the victim
surrounding,
They found they had captured—a scrub-kan
garoo.
Methinks I can call up the asinine change
Of expression which harassed their feature.'
that day,
And hear in the silence that fell o’er the range
Jim’s wild peal of laughter die slowly away.
Where’s Archie ?” you ask. “ Well I guess 1
am he.
’Neath the folds of my shirt, here, I still bear
the scar. ”
“And Kate?” “Married years ago—married
to me—
And as handsome as when she served drinks
in the bar.
Here s the landlord for orders—l’m dry with
this yabber;
Yours the same ;so is mine; bring your own;
how he winks !
You were asking just now what became of the
stabber,
Flash Jim ? Why, that’s he just gone out
for the drinks.”
T. H. ORD.

F. 0. Kendall, Henry Kendall’s eldest
son, contributes a well-written democratic rhyme
to the Workman (Sydney), and modestly asks
its insertion on the prestige of his father’s name.

In England the other day a young man
who had to undergo a slight operation was told to
take no solid food for eight hours before the event,
Nevertheless, he ate a hearty meal. He vomited
when the ether was given him and was fatally
choked by undigested food.
Bradlaugh’s paper, the National Re-
fokmek, is now edited by that able young man,
J. M. Robertson.
The late David Proudfoot, railway-con-
tractor, who died under chloroform at Sydney
recently, started business in New Zealand when but
17 years old. He had no capital, but he soon worked
his way up, and, before he was 25, was engaged
in contracts amounting to many thousands of
pounds. He was a born contractor.
Incident in the trial of Li Ki Hong, who
has been sentenced to death at Perth, W.A., for
the murder of another Chinee, known as Ah Gin.
Attorney General Burt: “Li?” Prisoner:
“ Um.” “ You savee what this gentleman,
Enright, say?” “Me savee.” “ You savee he
tell um this gentleman (pointing to Judge Wren-
fordsley) you chop um Chinaman’s head off?”
“No savee !” Hanged allee same.
p
That is a good old-fashioned yarn of the
clergyman who, preaching in a strange church,
asked to have a glass of water in the pulpit, and,
after the sermon, remarked to the clerk in the
vestry, “ That might have been gin-and-water,
John, for all the people could tell.” Taking the
duty again there next Sunday, he found, to his
horror, that it was gin-and-water: “ I took the
hint, sir—I took the hint,” quoth John from the
clerk’s desk below.
“THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at Sydney, N.8.W.)
The Unique Weekly-Australia's Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
“The Bulletin,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal ,
Is the only paper which has a consistent mid dis
tinctive Australian National Polity.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
Payment of Members.
One Person, one Vote.
State Revenue derived directly from the Land.
Complete Secularisation of State Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
The Bulletin denounces—
Religious Interference in Politics.
Foreign Titles.
The Chinese.
Imperial Federation.
The public eye rejects as uninteresting mere
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter which is not
of general human interest. The Bollotih was
started eleven years ago with no capital but brains>
has become a vast property, because it pos.
sesses a vitality lacked by the countless news-
paper-ventures in which Australian capitalists
have unavailingly spent large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after the
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class ) rinting
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which', throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress : it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the claims of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, aerainst the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the New Zealand
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based
Perhaps the strongest proof of The BuLumN’a
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact —
easily ascertainable from any postal official — that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

10th December, 1888.
To H. F. Kugelmann, Esq.
Dear Sir, —This is to certify that I, Patrick Keenan, was
taken ill about three years ago with a severe cold, which
turned into Bronchitis and became chronic. I consulted
more than one doctor, and during that time took a great
quantity of medicine without it doing me the slightest
good, but 1 became worse every day, wasting away until
/ became a perfect skeleton. About February last I began
to spit up blood, and what with the cough and Severe
pains in my left lung, and the quantity ,of bad matter I
was expectorating every day, I became very much alarmed.
Hearing cf your professional skill I consulted you, and
you told me you could cure me for a given sum. 1 agreed"
and went under your treatment in March last. At the
end of three months I was taken had , one morning, and
vomited about two pints of blood, matter and stringy
phlegm. From that time I commenced to improve, and
was gaining in weight every day. About'a month ago the
blood ceased coming, the cough disappeared, and the
lung healed completely, and thank God and your pro-
fessional skill, I am as well as ever.
P. KEENAN.
Declared and subscribed before me, one of Her Majesty’s
Justices of the Peace, for tlr Southern Bhiliwick, in the
County of Grant, this 10th da •'i December, 1888.
Peter S.mytiie, J.P.
